كتاب الوكالة باب عزل الوكيل
الوديعة لم ينعزل بعجزه وحجره كما في كافي الحاكم ولا تعود الوكالة بكتابة موكله وإذنه . وقد حصر المؤلف عزل وكيلهما بهما ويعزل الموكل أخذاً من عموم بطلانها بعزل الموكل، فأفاد أن المولى لو عزل وكيل العبد المأذون له لم ينعزل لأنه كالحجر الخاص. ولو أعتق العبد بعد ما وكله سيده أو طلقها ثلاثاً بعد ما وكلها لم ينعزلا ، وإن باع العبد فإن رضي المشتري أن يكون العبد على وكالته فهو وكيل وإن لم يرض بذلك لم يجبر على الوكالة؛ كذا في كافي الحاكم. وهو يقتضي أن توكيل عبد الغير موقوف على رضا السيد وقد سبق إطلاق جوازه لأنه لا عهدة عليه في ذلك إلا أن يقال إنه من باب استخدام عبد الغير وقد سئلت عن ناظر وكل وكيلاً في أمر الوقف ثم عزله القاضي هل ينعزل وكيله بعزله؟ فأجبت بأنه ينعزل أخذاً من قولهم هنا يشترط لدوامها ما يشترط لابتدائها والله أعلم.
قوله : (وتصرفه بنفسه أي يبطل بتصرف الموكل فيما وكل فيه لانقضاء الحاجة. أطلقه وهو مقيد بتصرف يعجز الوكيل عن التصرف معه كما لو وكله بإعتاق عبده أو بكتابته فأعتقه أو كاتبه الموكل بنفسه أو بتزويج امرأة أو بشراء شيء ففعل بنفسه أو بطلاق فطلقها الزوج ثلاثاً أو واحدة فانقضت عدتها أو بالخلع فخالعها بنفسه، وأما ما لا يعجز عنه فلا تبطل به كما لو طلقها واحدة والعدة باقية فللوكيل أن يطلقها أخرى ولو ارتد الزوج وقع طلاق الوكيل عليها ما دامت في العدة والحوقه بمنزلة موته ولو وكله بطلاقها فخالعها الزوج وقع طلاق الوكيل في عدتها، ولو وكل بالبيع فباعه الموكل ثم رد عليه بما هو فسخ فالوكيل على وكالته، وإن رد بما لا يكون فسخاً لا تعود الوكالة كما لو وكله في هبة شيء ثم وهبه الموكل ثم رجع في هبته لم يكن للوكيل الهبة، ولو وكله بالبيع ثم رهنه الموكل أو أجره فسلمه فهو على وكالته في ظاهر الرواية، ولو وكله أن يؤجر داره ثم أجرها الموكل بنفسه ثم انفسخت الإجارة يعود على وكالته ؛ كذا في القنية . وفي البزازية ولو وكله ببيع داره ثم بنى فيها فهو رجوع عنها عند الإمام ومحمد لأن التجصيص والوصية بمنزلة الوكالة، وكذا لو ببيع أرضه ثم غرس فيها بخلاف ما إذا وكله ببيع أرض وزرع فيبيع الوكيل الأرض دون الزرع لأن البناء والغرس يقصد بهما القرار لا الزرع أمره بشراء دار وهي أرض بيضاء فبنى فيها ليس له أن يشتريها بعده، ولو كانت مبنية فزاد فيها حائطاً أو جصصها له البيع. ببيع وصيفة وهي شابة فصارت عجوزاً فالوكالة على حالها بخلاف ما إذا أمره بشراء [؟] وكله وكله فالمشتري مرتهن قوله : ( عزل وكيلهما بهما) أي الأجر والعجز قوله : (إلا أن يقال الخ) إذا كان من باب الاستخدام لعبد الغير يتوقف على رضا سيده لأنه لا يملك منافعه تأمل.
قوله : (لأن التجصيص) هكذا في أغلب النسخ وفي نسخة لا التجصيص بلا النافية وقوله والوصية مبتدأ خبره ما بعده.
Book of Agency, Chapter of Dismissal of the Agent. The deposit is not dismissed by his inability and his restriction as in Kafi al-Hakim. And the agency does not return by the writing of his principal and his permission. The author restricted the dismissal of both agents with both of them. And the principal dismisses, taken from the generality of its nullification by the dismissal of the principal. So he indicated that if the master dismisses the agent of the permitted slave, he is not dismissed because it is like a special restriction. And if the slave is freed after his master appointed him, or he divorced her three times after he appointed her, they are not dismissed. And if the slave is sold, if the buyer is pleased that the slave remain on his agency, he is an agent; if he is not pleased, he is not forced upon agency; as in Kafi al-Hakim. And it requires that appointment of another's slave is suspended upon the satisfaction of the master, and previously the absolute permissibility has been mentioned because there is no liability upon him in that, except if it is said that it is from the category of using another's slave. And I was asked about a supervisor who appointed an agent in a waqf matter, then the judge dismissed him: is his agent dismissed by his dismissal? I answered that he is dismissed, taken from their statement here: 'What is required for its continuation is what is required for its commencement.' And Allah knows best. His statement: 'And his own action' meaning it is nullified by the action of the principal in what he was appointed for due to the end of need. He made it absolute, but it is restricted to an action that incapacitates the agent from acting with it, such as if he appointed him to free his slave or to write a contract of kitaba, and the principal himself freed or wrote the kitaba; or to marry a woman or buy something, and he did it himself; or to divorce, so the husband divorced her three times or once and her iddah ended; or to khul', so he khala'd her himself. As for what does not incapacitate, it is not nullified by it, such as if he divorced her once and the iddah remains, so the agent may divorce her another time. And if the husband apostatizes, the agent's divorce of her takes effect as long as she is in iddah, and his apostasy is like his death. And if he appointed him to divorce her, and the husband khala'd her, the agent's divorce takes effect in her iddah. And if he appointed him to sell, and the principal sold it, then he returned it in a way that is a rescission, the agent remains on his agency. And if he returned it in a way that is not a rescission, the agency does not return, such as if he appointed him to give something as a gift, and the principal gave it as a gift, then retracted his gift, the agent does not have the gift. And if he appointed him to sell, then the principal mortgaged it or leased it and delivered it, he remains on his agency in the apparent narration. And if he appointed him to lease his house, then the principal himself leased it, then the lease was rescinded, he returns to his agency; as in al-Qunyah. And in al-Bazzaziyyah: if he appointed him to sell his house, then he built in it, it is a retraction from it according to Imam and Muhammad, because plastering and bequest are like agency. Similarly, if he appointed him to sell his land, then he planted in it, unlike if he appointed him to sell land and crop, the agent sells the land without the crop, because building and planting intend permanence, not crop. He ordered him to buy a house, and it was empty land, so he built in it, he does not have the right to buy it afterwards. And if it was built, and he added a wall or plastered it, he has the right to sell. He appointed him to sell a slave-girl who was young, then she became old, the agency remains as it was, unlike if he ordered him to buy [???] and [???] so the buyer is a mortgagee. His statement: 'Dismissal of both agents with both' meaning the building and inability. His statement: 'except if it is said etc.' if it is from the category of using another's slave, it depends on the satisfaction of his master, because he does not own his benefits. Consider. His statement: 'Because plastering' is like this in most copies, and in a copy 'not plastering' with the negative la, and his statement 'and bequest' is the subject and its predicate follows.
Source pages p. 324

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq is a commentary on the matn of Kanz al-Daqa'iq in the chapter on dismissal of the agent. It discusses several rulings regarding when an agency is nullified. The passage begins by noting that a deposit is not dismissed by the agent's incapacity or restriction, as mentioned in Kafi al-Hakim. Also, agency does not automatically return if the principal writes or permits something. The author then addresses the dismissal by the principal. Importantly, the principal's dismissal of an agent appointed by a master over his slave is ineffective because it is like a special restriction (hajr khas). Similarly, if the master frees the slave or divorces the slave-wife after appointing them as agents, they are not dismissed. If the slave is sold, the buyer may choose to keep the slave as agent; if not, he cannot be forced. This implies that appointing another's slave as agent is suspended on the master's consent. The passage then discusses the principal's own action: the agency is nullified if the principal performs the task himself, but only if that action prevents the agent from acting. For example, if the principal frees the slave, writes kitaba, marries, buys, or divorces in a way that ends the need, the agency ends. If the principal divorces once but iddah remains, the agent can still divorce another time. Apostasy of the husband is like death. Various examples follow: if the principal sells and then the sale is rescinded in a way that is a true rescission (faskh), the agent remains; if it is not a true rescission, agency does not return. Mortgaging or leasing does not nullify agency in the apparent narration. Building or plastering by the principal is considered a retraction according to Imam Abu Hanifa and Muhammad al-Shaybani. However, if the land was empty and the principal built, the agent cannot buy it afterwards; if it was built and the principal added, the agent may still sell. Change in the condition of the slave-girl (becoming old) does not nullify agency. The passage ends with textual notes on the variant reading of 'al-tajsis'.

Key Terms
العتق (al-'itq): Manumission; freeing a slave; does not nullify agency according to this passage.
الوقف (al-waqf): Waqf; a charitable endowment. The supervisor (nazir) is the manager.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A slave agent is sold to a new owner. According to the passage, the agency:
A) Is automatically nullified because the agent is sold.
B) Continues only if the new owner consents.
C) Continues regardless of the new owner's wishes.
D) Is nullified only if the agent was appointed by the previous owner for a specific task.

Key Takeaway
Changes in agent's status (freedom, divorce) do not automatically nullify agency; sale requires new principal's consent.

Change of Subject Matter in Agency and Sale with Option
Opening ruling on wakalah and sale with option

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on change of subject matter in wakalah and distinguish it from sale with option.

Core Idea
Any change in the subject matter voids agency, but not a sale with option.

What happens to a contract when the very thing it deals with changes form? This lesson examines the Hanafi position on transformation in two distinct settings: agency (wakalah) and sale with option (bay' al-khiyar). You will see that strict qiyas applies in wakalah, while flexibility is shown in option contracts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
Find in the Arabic passage the ruling when an agent takes saweeq or sesame and presses it into oil. Note that the agency is invalidated (batalat al-wakalah).
The act is pressing the commodity into a different form.
2
Contrast with sale with option
Read the part about sale with option: if change occurs during the option period, it does not invalidate the sale or the option. Understand why the ruling differs.
3
Identify the underlying principle
Agency is a contract of representation that is sensitive to the specific subject matter; any change destroys the connection. Sale with option is a binding contract that survives changes during the option period.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الوكالة باب عزل الوكيل سويق فلته أو سمسم فعصره فصار دهناً حيث تبطل الوكالة وفي البيع لا ا هـ. وفي وصايا الخانية : ولو قال أوصيت بهذه الكفري الذي في نخلتي فصار بسراً قبل موت الموصي بطلت الوصية، ولو قال أوصيت بهذا الرطب الذي في نخلتي فصار تمراً قبل موت الموصي في القياس تبطل الوصية ولا تبطل استحساناً، ولو قال أوصيت بعنبي هذا لفلان فصار زبيباً قبل موت الموصي بطلت الوصية قياساً واستحساناً، ولو قال أوصيت بزرعي هذا لفلان وهو بقل فصار حنطة أو شعيراً قبل موت الموصي بطلت الوصية وفي الوكالة إذا تغير في هذا كله بطلت الوكالة، وفي البيع بشرط الخيار إذا تغير في أيام الخيار لا يبطل البيع ولا الخيار ا هـ . وفي البدائع : إذا باع الموكل ماوكل ببيعه ولم يعلم الوكيل فباعه وقبض الثمن فهلك في يده ومات العبد قبل التسليم ورجع المشتري على الوكيل رجع الوكيل على الموكل، وكذا لو دبره أو أعتقه أو استحق أو كان حر الأصل لأنه صار مغروراً من جهة ولو مات الموكل أو جن لا يرجع لعدم الغرور والوكيل بقبض الدين لو قبضه وهلك في يده بعد ما وهبه الموكل للمديون ولم يعلم الوكيل لم يضمن وتمامه فيه ا هـ . والله أعلم.
Book of Agency, Chapter on Dismissal of Agent: If he [the agent] takes saweeq or sesame and presses it and it becomes oil, the agency is invalidated, but in a sale [with option] it is not invalidated. And in Wasiyyah from al-Khaniyyah: If he said, 'I bequeath these unripe dates that are on my palm tree' and they become ripe before the testator's death, the bequest is invalidated. If he said, 'I bequeath this ripe date that is on my palm tree' and it becomes dry dates before the testator's death, by qiyas the bequest is invalidated, but by istihsan it is not invalidated. If he said, 'I bequeath these grapes to so-and-so' and they become raisins before the testator's death, the bequest is invalidated both by qiyas and istihsan. If he said, 'I bequeath this crop to so-and-so' while it is green and it becomes wheat or barley before the testator's death, the bequest is invalidated. And in agency, if change occurs in all these cases, the agency is invalidated. And in a sale with a condition of option, if change occurs during the option days, it does not invalidate the sale nor the option. End. And in al-Bada'i: If the principal sells what he had appointed the agent to sell without informing the agent, and the agent sells it and takes the price and it perishes in his hand, and the slave dies before delivery, and the buyer recourse against the agent, the agent recourse against the principal. Likewise if he [the principal] made him a mudabbar or freed him or he was claimed by another or was originally free, because he became deceived from his side. But if the principal died or became insane, he does not recourse due to lack of deception. And the agent for collecting a debt, if he collects it and it perishes in his hand after the principal had donated it to the debtor without the agent's knowledge, he is not liable. End. And God knows best.
Source pages p. 325

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq collects several rulings regarding the effect of change in the subject matter on legal dispositions. The first ruling concerns agency: if the commodity changes form (e.g., sesame pressed into oil), the agency becomes void. However, if it is a sale with a condition of option (khiyar), change during the option period does not void the sale or the option. The second part from al-Wasaya al-Khaniyyah deals with bequests: if the specified fruit changes before the testator's death, the ruling varies. For unripe dates becoming ripe, qiyas voids but istihsan upholds it because the change is considered incomplete. For grapes becoming raisins, both qiyas and istihsan void it because the change is complete. For green crops becoming wheat, both void it. The general rule stated is that any change in the subject matter voids the agency (wakalah). The third part from al-Bada'i discusses cases where the principal sells the item without informing the agent: if the agent then sells it and the price is lost, the agent can recover from the principal because he was deceived (maghrur). But if the principal dies or becomes insane, the agent cannot recover because there is no deception. Finally, if an agent appointed to collect a debt collects it and it perishes, but the principal had already donated the debt to the debtor without the agent's knowledge, the agent is not liable. These rulings illustrate the Hanafi method of preferring istihsan over strict qiyas in certain circumstances to achieve equitable outcomes.

Key Terms
وكالة (wakālah): A contract of agency where one person appoints another to act on his behalf in a legal matter.
خيار (khiyār): An option clause in a contract allowing one or both parties to cancel within a specified period.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what happens to agency if the agent presses sesame into oil?
A) The agency remains valid because oil is still a valuable commodity.
B) The agency is invalidated.
C) The agency becomes suspended until the principal approves.
D) The agent must pay compensation.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, any complete change in the subject matter voids an agency contract, but a sale with option survives such change during the option period.

Change in Subject Matter in Bequests: Qiyas vs. Istihsan
Rulings in bequests with comparison of qiyas and istihsan

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi distinction between qiyas and istihsan to cases of transformation in bequests (wasiyyah).

Core Idea
Istihsan saves a bequest from voidity when the change is incomplete, but not when it is complete.

When a testator bequeaths a specific fruit, does the bequest become void if the fruit changes form before death? The Hanafi school uses both qiyas and istihsan to answer, arriving at nuanced rulings. This lesson explores those rulings and the reasoning behind them.

Guided Path
1
Read the cases from al-Khaniyyah
Identify the four bequest scenarios: unripe dates becoming ripe, ripe dates becoming dry, grapes becoming raisins, and green crops becoming wheat/barley.
2
Note the ruling by qiyas for each
In all four cases, qiyas (strict analogy) says the bequest is void because the subject matter at death differs from what was bequeathed.
Qiyas is based on the principle that the bequest must be in existence as described at the time of death.
3
Identify where istihsan overrides
Only the case of ripe dates becoming dry is saved by istihsan. Understand the reason: the change from ripe date to dry date is considered incomplete (both are dates, just different moisture).
Change is incomplete when the essence remains; change is complete when the new form is a different commodity (grape→raisin, green crop→grain).
4
Apply the general rule for wakalah
The passage also states that in agency, any change voids the agency — no istihsan exception.
The stricter rule for agency contrasts with the flexibility in bequests.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الوكالة باب عزل الوكيل سويق فلته أو سمسم فعصره فصار دهناً حيث تبطل الوكالة وفي البيع لا ا هـ. وفي وصايا الخانية : ولو قال أوصيت بهذه الكفري الذي في نخلتي فصار بسراً قبل موت الموصي بطلت الوصية، ولو قال أوصيت بهذا الرطب الذي في نخلتي فصار تمراً قبل موت الموصي في القياس تبطل الوصية ولا تبطل استحساناً، ولو قال أوصيت بعنبي هذا لفلان فصار زبيباً قبل موت الموصي بطلت الوصية قياساً واستحساناً، ولو قال أوصيت بزرعي هذا لفلان وهو بقل فصار حنطة أو شعيراً قبل موت الموصي بطلت الوصية وفي الوكالة إذا تغير في هذا كله بطلت الوكالة، وفي البيع بشرط الخيار إذا تغير في أيام الخيار لا يبطل البيع ولا الخيار ا هـ . وفي البدائع : إذا باع الموكل ماوكل ببيعه ولم يعلم الوكيل فباعه وقبض الثمن فهلك في يده ومات العبد قبل التسليم ورجع المشتري على الوكيل رجع الوكيل على الموكل، وكذا لو دبره أو أعتقه أو استحق أو كان حر الأصل لأنه صار مغروراً من جهة ولو مات الموكل أو جن لا يرجع لعدم الغرور والوكيل بقبض الدين لو قبضه وهلك في يده بعد ما وهبه الموكل للمديون ولم يعلم الوكيل لم يضمن وتمامه فيه ا هـ . والله أعلم.
Book of Agency, Chapter on Dismissal of Agent: If he [the agent] takes saweeq or sesame and presses it and it becomes oil, the agency is invalidated, but in a sale [with option] it is not invalidated. And in Wasiyyah from al-Khaniyyah: If he said, 'I bequeath these unripe dates that are on my palm tree' and they become ripe before the testator's death, the bequest is invalidated. If he said, 'I bequeath these grapes to so-and-so' and they become raisins before the testator's death, the bequest is invalidated both by qiyas and istihsan. If he said, 'I bequeath this crop to so-and-so' while it is green and it becomes wheat or barley before the testator's death, the bequest is invalidated. And in agency, if change occurs in all these cases, the agency is invalidated. And in a sale with a condition of option, if change occurs during the option days, it does not invalidate the sale nor the option. End. Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq collects several rulings regarding the effect of change in the subject matter on legal dispositions. The first ruling concerns agency: if the commodity changes form (e.g., sesame pressed into oil), the agency becomes void. However, if it is a sale with a condition of option (khiyar), change during the option period does not void the sale or the option. The second part from al-Wasaya al-Khaniyyah deals with bequests: if the specified fruit changes before the testator's death, the ruling varies. For unripe dates becoming ripe, qiyas voids but istihsan upholds it because the change is considered incomplete. For grapes becoming raisins, both qiyas and istihsan void it because the change is complete. For green crops becoming wheat, both void it. The general rule stated is that any change in the subject matter voids the agency (wakalah). The third part from al-Bada'i discusses cases where the principal sells the item without informing the agent: if the agent then sells it and the price is lost, the agent can recover from the principal because he was deceived (maghrur). But if the principal dies or becomes insane, the agent cannot recover because there is no deception. Finally, if an agent appointed to collect a debt collects it and it perishes, but the principal had already donated the debt to the debtor without the agent's knowledge, the agent is not liable. These rulings illustrate the Hanafi method of preferring istihsan over strict qiyas in certain circumstances to achieve equitable outcomes.

Key Terms
قياس (qiyās): Analogical reasoning that derives a ruling for a new case from an original case due to shared 'illa.
استحسان (istiḥsān): Preferring one ruling over another due to a stronger evidence or equitable consideration.
وصية (waṣiyyah): A bequest or will made by a person for after their death.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the passage, which bequest is saved by istihsan?
A) Unripe dates becoming ripe
B) Ripe dates becoming dry
C) Grapes becoming raisins
D) Green crop becoming wheat

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, istihsan may preserve a bequest when the subject matter undergoes an incomplete change, while complete changes void the bequest by both qiyas and istihsan.

Agent's Liability When Principal Changes Subject Matter Without Notice
Rulings on agent's liability and recourse from al-Bada'i

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the agent's right of recourse when the principal disposes of the subject matter without informing the agent, and distinguish cases where recourse is available from those where it is not.

Core Idea
An agent who suffers loss due to the principal's undisclosed disposition can recover compensation, unless the principal dies or becomes insane.

What happens if the principal sells the item the agent was appointed to sell, without telling the agent, and the agent subsequently sells it to a third party? This lesson from al-Bada'i explores the agent's liability and rights of recourse, including the concept of deception (ghurur).

Guided Path
1
Understand the base scenario
The agent is appointed to sell a slave. The principal sells the slave privately without informing the agent. The agent then sells the same slave to a buyer, takes the price, but the price perishes in his hand. The slave dies before delivery, so the buyer recourses against the agent.
The agent is liable to the buyer because he sold something he didn't own.
2
Identify the agent's right of recourse
The agent can recover from the principal because he was deceived (maghrur) — the principal's failure to inform caused the loss.
This holds if the principal sold, made the slave a mudabbar, freed him, or the slave was claimed by another or found to be free. In all these, the agent is deceived.
3
Note exceptions where recourse is not available
If the principal dies or becomes insane before the agent's sale, the agent cannot recourse because there is no deception (ghurur) — the principal did not actively mislead.
4
Consider the case of agent for debt collection
If an agent is appointed to collect a debt, and the principal later donates the debt to the debtor without informing the agent, and the agent collects and loses the money, the agent is not liable because he acted in ignorance.
The agent is considered excused because he had no knowledge of the donation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الوكالة باب عزل الوكيل سويق فلته أو سمسم فعصره فصار دهناً حيث تبطل الوكالة وفي البيع لا ا هـ. وفي وصايا الخانية : ولو قال أوصيت بهذه الكفري الذي في نخلتي فصار بسراً قبل موت الموصي بطلت الوصية، ولو قال أوصيت بهذا الرطب الذي في نخلتي فصار تمراً قبل موت الموصي في القياس تبطل الوصية ولا تبطل استحساناً، ولو قال أوصيت بعنبي هذا لفلان فصار زبيباً قبل موت الموصي بطلت الوصية قياساً واستحساناً، ولو قال أوصيت بزرعي هذا لفلان وهو بقل فصار حنطة أو شعيراً قبل موت الموصي بطلت الوصية وفي الوكالة إذا تغير في هذا كله بطلت الوكالة، وفي البيع بشرط الخيار إذا تغير في أيام الخيار لا يبطل البيع ولا الخيار ا هـ . وفي البدائع : إذا باع الموكل ماوكل ببيعه ولم يعلم الوكيل فباعه وقبض الثمن فهلك في يده ومات العبد قبل التسليم ورجع المشتري على الوكيل رجع الوكيل على الموكل، وكذا لو دبره أو أعتقه أو استحق أو كان حر الأصل لأنه صار مغروراً من جهة ولو مات الموكل أو جن لا يرجع لعدم الغرور والوكيل بقبض الدين لو قبضه وهلك في يده بعد ما وهبه الموكل للمديون ولم يعلم الوكيل لم يضمن وتمامه فيه ا هـ . والله أعلم.
Book of Agency, Chapter on Dismissal of Agent: If he [the agent] takes saweeq or sesame and presses it and it becomes oil, the agency is invalidated, but in a sale [with option] it is not invalidated. And in Wasiyyah from al-Khaniyyah: If he said, 'I bequeath these unripe dates that are on my palm tree' and they become ripe before the testator's death, the bequest is invalidated. If he said, 'I bequeath this ripe date that is on my palm tree' and it becomes dry dates before the testator's death, by qiyas the bequest is invalidated, but by istihsan it is not invalidated. If he said, 'I bequeath these grapes to so-and-so' and they become raisins before the testator's death, the bequest is invalidated both by qiyas and istihsan. If he said, 'I bequeath this crop to so-and-so' while it is green and it becomes wheat or barley before the testator's death, the bequest is invalidated. And in agency, if change occurs in all these cases, the agency is invalidated. And in a sale with a condition of option, if change occurs during the option days, it does not invalidate the sale nor the option. End. And in al-Bada'i: If the principal sells what he had appointed the agent to sell without informing the agent, and the agent sells it and takes the price and it perishes in his hand, and the slave dies before delivery, and the buyer recourse against the agent, the agent recourse against the principal. Likewise if he [the principal] made him a mudabbar or freed him or he was claimed by another or was originally free, because he became deceived from his side. But if the principal died or became insane, he does not recourse due to lack of deception. And the agent for collecting a debt, if he collects it and it perishes in his hand after the principal had donated it to the debtor without the agent's knowledge, he is not liable. End. And God knows best.
Source pages p. 325

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq collects several rulings regarding the effect of change in the subject matter on legal dispositions. The first ruling concerns agency: if the commodity changes form (e.g., sesame pressed into oil), the agency becomes void. However, if it is a sale with a condition of option (khiyar), change during the option period does not void the sale or the option. The second part from al-Wasaya al-Khaniyyah deals with bequests: if the specified fruit changes before the testator's death, the ruling varies. For unripe dates becoming ripe, qiyas voids but istihsan upholds it because the change is considered incomplete. For grapes becoming raisins, both qiyas and istihsan void it because the change is complete. For green crops becoming wheat, both void it. The general rule stated is that any change in the subject matter voids the agency (wakalah). The third part from al-Bada'i discusses cases where the principal sells the item without informing the agent: if the agent then sells it and the price is lost, the agent can recover from the principal because he was deceived (maghrur). But if the principal dies or becomes insane, the agent cannot recover because there is no deception. Finally, if an agent appointed to collect a debt collects it and it perishes, but the principal had already donated the debt to the debtor without the agent's knowledge, the agent is not liable. These rulings illustrate the Hanafi method of preferring istihsan over strict qiyas in certain circumstances to achieve equitable outcomes.

Key Terms
غرور (ghurūr): Deception or misrepresentation that causes another to act to his detriment.
مدبر (mudabbir): A slave who is promised freedom upon the master's death.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, when does the agent NOT have recourse against the principal?
A) When the principal sells the item
B) When the principal makes the slave mudabbar
C) When the principal dies
D) When the principal frees the slave

Key Takeaway
An agent who suffers loss due to the principal's undisclosed disposition has a right of recourse based on ghurur, unless the principal dies or becomes insane, negating deception.

The Eight Conditions for a Valid Legal Claim (Da'wa) in Hanafi Fiqh
Enumerating eight conditions for judicial acceptance of a claim.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the eight conditions for a valid claim and apply them to determine whether a claim is accepted in a Hanafi court.

Core Idea
A claim must satisfy eight conditions regarding capacity, possibility, procedure, and consistency to be heard.

The Hanafi school has enumerated precise conditions that must be met for a legal claim to be entertained by a judge. These conditions ensure that only serious, plausible, and properly presented claims occupy the court's time. This lesson walks through each condition as outlined in the classical Hanafi texts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the parties and their capacity
Check that both the claimant and the defendant are sane. Also, the claimant must be identifiable (known to the court).
Insanity or unknown identity invalidates the claim.
2
Evaluate the subject matter
Is the claim something that is possible in itself? Distinguish between intellectual impossibility (e.g., impossible paternity) and customary impossibility (e.g., a poor person claiming huge wealth). Intellectual impossibility makes the claim void; the ruling on customary impossibility is that it is likely not heard.
The author notes he has not seen a decisive text for customary impossibility but the apparent ruling is rejection.
3
Check procedural conditions
Ensure the claim is made by the claimant in his own words (not through an agent without the opponent's consent), it is made in the court session (before the judge), and the opponent is present (unless a judicial writ is obtained).
These ensure proper procedure and the opponent's right to respond.
4
Ensure consistency
The claimant must not have made prior statements that contradict the current claim. Exceptions exist for lineage and freedom claims.
Contradiction indicates dishonesty or confusion.
5
Verify the claimant's demand
The claimant must be demanding something that is legally obligatory upon the opponent. For example, a claim of agency against a present principal is invalid because the principal can dismiss the agent at any time.
The demand must be enforceable.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الرابع في شروطها المصححة لها فمنها عقل المدعي والمدعى عليه، ومنها معلومية المدعي كما سيأتي بيانه ومنها كون المدعي مما يحتمل الثبوت فدعوى ما يستحيل وجوده باطلة كقوله لمن لا يولد مثله لمثله هذا ابني أو قال ذلك لمعروف النسب، ولم أر حكم المستحيل عادة كدعوى فقير أموالاً عظيمة على غني أنه غصبها منه، والظاهر عدم سماعها ثم كتبت بعد ذلك في آخر باب التحالف ما يفيده فليراجع . ومنها كونها بلسان المدعي فلا تصح بلسان وكيله إلا برضا خصمه عند الإمام إذا لم يكن به عذر. ومنها مجلس القضاء فلا تسمع هي والشهادة إلا بين يدي الحاكم. ومنها حضرة الخصم فلا يسمعان إلا على خصم حاضر إلا إذا التمس المدعي ذلك بالكتاب الحكمي للقضاء. ومنها عدم التناقض في الدعوى إلا في النسب والحرية وهو أن لا يسبق منه ما يناقض دعواه كما لو أقر بالملك له ثم ادعى الشراء منه قبله لا بعده أو مطلقاً، وهذه السبعة في البدائع. ومنها كون المدعي ملزماً على الخصم فلا تصح دعوى التوكيل على موكله الحاضر لإمكان عزله كما في العناية
The fourth [topic] is regarding its conditions that render it valid. Among them are: sanity of the claimant and the defendant; knowledge of the claimant (as will be explained); that the claim be of something capable of being proven – so a claim for something impossible is void, like someone saying to a person who cannot have a child like him 'this is my son', or saying that about someone of known lineage. And I have not seen the ruling for what is customarily impossible, like a poor person claiming huge sums of wealth from a rich person saying he had usurped it from him. The apparent [ruling] is that it is not heard. Then it was written afterwards at the end of the chapter of Tahaluf what benefits it – let it be referred to. Among them: it must be in the claimant's own tongue – it is not valid in the tongue of his agent except with the consent of the opponent according to Imam [Abu Hanifa] if there is no excuse. Among them: the court session – the claim and testimony are only heard before the judge. Among them: presence of the opponent – they are not heard except against a present opponent, unless the claimant requests that via a judicial writ for judgment. Among them: absence of contradiction in the claim except in lineage and freedom – meaning that there should be nothing preceding that contradicts his claim, as if he had acknowledged ownership [for the opponent] and then claimed purchase from him before [the acknowledgment], not after, or absolutely. These seven are in al-Bada'i'. And among them: that the claimant be [making a claim that has] obligation upon the opponent – so a claim of agency against his present principal is not valid because he can be dismissed, as in al-'Inayah.
Source pages p. 327

Commentary
This passage enumerates the conditions (shurut) that must be present for a legal claim (da'wa) to be accepted for hearing by a judge according to the Hanafi school. The author lists eight conditions, seven from al-Bada'i' and an eighth from al-'Inayah. The conditions are: (1) sanity of both parties; (2) the claimant must be known (i.e., identifiable); (3) the subject matter must be capable of proof – this excludes claims that are intellectually impossible (like paternity of a child who cannot be one's son) and customarily impossible (like a beggar claiming a vast loan); (4) the claim must be uttered by the claimant himself, not through an agent without the opponent's consent; (5) it must be made in the presence of the judge (court session); (6) the opponent must be present; (7) there should be no contradiction in the claim (e.g., acknowledging ownership then claiming purchase from that person); (8) the claimant must be demanding something obligatory upon the opponent (so a claim of agency against a principal who can dismiss the agent is not valid). The author notes that the ruling on customarily impossible claims is not explicitly found in earlier sources, but the apparent position is that such claims are not entertained.

Key Terms
دعوى (daʿwā): A legal claim by which one party asserts a right against another before a judge.
مدعي (muddaʿī): The claimant who brings the case to court.
مستحيل عادة (mustaḥīl ʿādatan): Something that is customarily impossible, i.e., extremely unlikely to occur in normal circumstances.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) The claim is accepted because the law does not consider custom.
B) The claim is heard but the poor person must bring witnesses.
C) The claim is rejected because it is customarily impossible.
D) The claim is accepted if the rich person admits.

Key Takeaway
A claim must satisfy eight conditions—including sanity, possibility, proper procedure, and consistency—to be heard in a Hanafi court; otherwise, it is void or not entertained.

Ruling, Cause, and Purpose of a Claim (Da'wa)
Explaining the legal effect, underlying cause, and objective of the claim procedure

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling, cause, and purpose of a claim (da'wa) according to the Hanafi school, and explain the procedural obligation of the defendant to respond.

Core Idea
A claim's ruling is the defendant's wajib to respond; its cause is leftover transaction obligations; its purpose is dispute resolution.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq unpacks three fundamental dimensions of da'wa (claim) in Hanafi fiqh: its legal ruling (hukm), its underlying cause (sabab), and its legislative purpose (maqsud). Understanding these helps you see why the judge summons the defendant and why the system exists at all. The author also clarifies a subtle distinction between a condition (shart) and a ruling (hukm) regarding the defendant's presence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling (hukm) of a claim
The text states clearly: 'wa huwa wujub al-jawab 'ala al-mudda'a 'alayh' – the ruling is the obligation upon the defendant to answer. This is a wajib, not fard, because the evidence is zanni (probable).
Wujub al-jawab is the core hukm; attendance is a condition, not part of the ruling itself.
2
Trace the cause (sabab) of a claim
The cause is the potential remaining rights from transactions (ta'alliq al-baqa' al-muqaddar bi-ta'ati al-mu'amalat). Claims arise because parties may still owe each other from dealings.
This explains why da'wa exists: transactions create rights that may need enforcement.
3
Understand the purpose (maqsud) of legislation
The purpose is not the claim itself but its resolution through judgment to prevent likely corruption (fitna). The court ends disputes.
The maqsud is daf' al-fasad – protecting societal order.
4
Examine the procedural details
The passage details how a judge summons the defendant: if in the city or near, summon directly; if far, the claimant must bring evidence before the judge sends for him. This shows the link between ruling and procedure.
This also touches on the disagreement about whether attendance is a ruling or condition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الخامس في حكمها وهو وجوب الجواب على المدعى عليه، واقتصر عليه في الكافي وزاد الشارح وجوب الحضور على الخصم وفيه نظر، لأن حضوره شرطها كما قدمناه فكيف يكون وجوبه حكمها المتأخر عنها؟ أبعد منه وحاصله كما في منية المفتي أن المدعي إذا طلب من القاضي إحضار الخصم أحضره بمجرد الدعوى إن كان في المصر أو كان قريباً بحيث لو أجاب يبيت في منزله، وإن كان قيل يأمره بإقامة البينة على موافقة دعواه لإحضار خصمه والمستور في هذا يكفي، فإذا أقام يأمر إنساناً ليحضر خصمه، وقيل يحلفه القاضي؛ فإن نكل أقامه عن مجلسه، وإن حلف يأمر بإحضاره ا.هـ. وقدمنا في أدب القاضي حكم ما إذا امتنع عن الحضور وأجرة الرسول لإحضاره وما إذا اختفى في بيته وحكم الهجوم عليه السادس في سببها قال في العناية : إنه تعلق البقاء المقدر بتعاطي المعاملات لأن المدعي إما أن يكون راجعاً إلى النوع أو بأن هذا إنما يكون من جانب المدعى عليه لدفع الدعوى أي فليس بدعوى، وأيضاً إذا علم أن الديون تقضى بأمثالها فالإيفاء دعوى دين والإبراء دعوى تمليك معنى اهـ. قوله : ( ولم أر حكم المستحيل عادة الخ) قال العلامة ابن الغرس في الفواكه البدرية: ومن إلى الشخص السابع في المقصود من شرعيتها قال في العناية وشرعيتها ليست لذاتها بل من حيث انقطاعها بالقضاء دفعاً للفساد المظنون ببقائها ا هـ
The fifth [topic] is its ruling: it is obligatory upon the defendant to respond. Al-Kafi limited it to that, but the commentator added the obligation of the opponent to attend, which is debatable because his presence is a condition [of the claim] as we stated earlier – so how can its obligation be its ruling that is subsequent to it? It is more appropriate [to say] as in Minyat al-Mufti: that if the claimant asks the judge to bring the opponent, he brings him by virtue of the claim if he is in the city or near enough that he could return home by nightfall. But if [the opponent] is far, the judge orders [the claimant] to bring evidence supporting his claim to bring his opponent, and the [claimant's being] veiled [in integrity] suffices for this. When he brings evidence, he orders a person to bring his opponent. Some say the judge administers an oath to him; if he refuses, the judge expels him from the court; if he swears, he orders the opponent to be brought. This is the end of the quote. And we have previously presented in Adab al-Qadi the ruling when he refuses to attend, the fee of the messenger to bring him, and when he hides in his house, and the ruling on raiding it. The sixth [topic] is its cause. It is said in al-'Inayah: it is the attachment of the estimated remaining [of time] by engaging in transactions, because the claimant either refers to the type or because this [claim] comes from the side of the defendant to repel the claim – meaning it is not a claim. Also, if it is known that debts are paid by their equivalents, then payment invokes the claim of a debt and discharge invokes the claim of a transfer of ownership in meaning. He said: 'And I have not seen the ruling for customary impossibility' – al-'Allamah Ibn al-Ghars said in al-Fawakih al-Badriyyah: ... to the seventh [topic] regarding the objective of its legislation. It is said in al-'Inayah: its legislation is not for itself, but rather for its termination by judgment to prevent the likely corruption from its continuation.
Source pages p. 327

Commentary
This passage covers three separate legal aspects of da'wa: its ruling (hukm), its cause (sabab), and its purpose (maqsud). The ruling is that it is wajib (obligatory) for the defendant to respond. The commentator adds that the opponent's presence is also obligatory, but the author refutes this because presence is a condition, not a ruling. The procedure for summoning the defendant is explained from Minyat al-Mufti. The cause of the claim is the potential for residual attachment from transactions – claims arise from dealings. The purpose of the entire institution of claims is not the claim itself but its resolution by judgment to prevent fitna. The inserted note from al-Fawakih al-Badriyyah discusses the ruling on customarily impossible claims, which was earlier mentioned as not found explicitly.

Key Terms
وجوب الجواب (wujūb al-jawāb): The obligation of the defendant to respond to the claim in court.
سبب الدعوى (sabab al-daʿwā): The legal cause that gives rise to a claim; here, it is the possibility of leftover obligations from transactions.
مقصود الشرعية (maqṣūd al-sharʿiyyah): The objective of the legislation: to resolve disputes and prevent corruption.
شَرْط (sharṭ): A condition that must be present for the claim to be valid; presence of the defendant is a shart, not a hukm.
حُكْم (ḥukm): The legal ruling flowing from the claim: wujub al-jawab.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) It is wajib upon the defendant to respond.

D) The judge must investigate independently.

Key Takeaway
The claim's ruling is the defendant's wajib to answer; its cause is lingering transaction ties; its purpose is to end disputes and prevent corruption.

Self-Help in Hudud, Qisas, and Ta'zir
Rulings on self-help in criminal and disciplinary matters.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on self-enforcement of hadd qadhf, qisas, and ta'zir, and identify the conditions under which self-help is permitted or prohibited.

Core Idea
Self-help is prohibited for Allah's rights but permissible for individual rights like qisas and ta'zir under strict conditions.

The Hanafi school provides detailed guidance on when a person may enforce their own right without judicial intervention. This lesson examines the rulings for hudud, qisas, and ta'zir, highlighting the distinction between Allah's rights and individual rights.

Guided Path
1
Identify the type of right
Determine whether the right is a prescribed hadd (e.g., qadhf), retribution (qisas), or discretionary punishment (ta'zir).
2
Assess the predominant aspect
For hadd, if it primarily involves Allah's right (as in qadhf), self-help is prohibited. For qisas, it is an individual right, so the wali may act.
3
Apply the specific conditions
For qisas: allowed only with a sword, and only by the wali. For ta'zir: both parties may be punished, starting with the aggressor. For insult: victim may respond in kind, but better to leave it.
4
Note the Hanafi position on husband's discipline
A husband may discipline his wife as part of his right, but this is a specific case of self-help.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولم يذكر الشارحون هنا حكم استيفاء ذي الحق حقه من الغير بلا قضاء، وأحببت جمعه هنا من مواضعه تكثيراً للفوائد وتيسيراً على طالبيها فإن كان الحق حد قذف فلا يستوفيه بنفسه لأن فيه حق الله تعالى اتفاقاً، والأصح أن الغالب فيه حقه تعالى فلا يستوفيه إلا من يقيم الحدود ولكن بطلب المقذوف كما بيناه في بابه. وإن كان قصاصاً فقال في جنايات البزازية : قتل الرجل عمداً وله ولي له أن يقتص بالسيف قضى به أو لا، ويضرب علاوته ولو رام قتله بغير سيف منع، وإن فعل عزر لكن لا يضمن لاستيفائه حقه ا هـ . وإن كان تعزيراً ففي حدود القنية : ضرب غيره بغير حق وضربه المضروب أيضاً أنهما يعزران ويبدأ بإقامة التعزير بالبادئ منهما لأنه أظلم والوجوب عليه أسبق ا هـ . وأما إذا شتمه فله أن يقول له مثله والأولى تركه كما قدمناه في محله وقالوا للزوج أن يؤدب زوجته. وفي جامع الفصولين من التحليف : ومن عليه التعزير لو مكن صاحب الحق منه أقامه ا وإن كان عيناً ففي إجارة القنية : ولو غاب المستأجر بعد السنة ولم يسلم المفتاح إلى الآخر فله أن يتخذ له مفتاحاً آخر، ولو أجره من غيره بغير إذن الحاكم جاز ا هـ . وقد صارت حادثة الفتوى مضت المدة وغاب المستأجر وترك متاعه في الدار فأفتيت بأن له أن يفتح الدار ويسكن فيها، وأما المتاع فيجعله في ناحية إلى حضور صاحبه ولا يتوقف الفتح على إذن القاضي أخذاً مما في القنية وفي غصب منية المفتي : أخذت أغصان شجرة إنسان هواء دار آخر فقطع رب الدار الأغصان، فإن كانت الأغصان بحالة يمكن لصاحبها إن يشدها بحبل ويفرغ هواء داره ضمن القاطع، وإن لم يمكن لا يضمن إذا قطع من موضع لو رفع إلى الحاكم أمر بالقطع من ذلك الموضع ا هـ. وإن كان ديناً ففي مداينات القنية : رب الدين إذا ظفر من جنس حقه من مال المديون على صفته فله أخذه بغير رضاه ولا يأخذ خلاف جنسه كالدراهم والدنانير، وعند الشافعي له أخذه بقدر قيمته، وعن أبي بكر الرازي له أخذ الدنانير بالدراهم، وكذا أخذ الدراهم بالدنانير استحساناً
The commentators did not mention here the ruling on a right-holder taking his right from another without litigation, and I liked to collect it here from its places to multiply benefits and make it easy for seekers. If the right is the Hadd of slander (qadhf), then he does not exact it himself because it involves the right of Allah most high by agreement, and the sounder view is that the predominant aspect is the right of Allah, so only the one who executes the prescribed punishments can exact it, but upon request of the one slandered, as we explained in its chapter. If it is retribution (qisas), then it is stated in Jinaayat al-Bazaziyyah: if a man is killed intentionally and he has a wali, he may exact retribution with a sword whether or not a judgment was passed; and he strikes his neck. If he intends to kill him with something other than a sword, he is prevented; if he does it, he is subject to ta'zir but does not incur liability because he took his right. If it is ta'zir, it is stated in Hudud al-Qunyah: if a person hits another wrongfully and the one hit also hits him, then both are subject to ta'zir, and the ta'zir is begun with the aggressor because he is more unjust and the obligation is earlier upon him. As for if he insults him, he may say the like back to him, but it is better to leave it as we have presented in its place. And they said: a husband may discipline his wife. And in Jami' al-Fusoolayn from al-Tahlif: if a person is liable for ta'zir and he permits the right-holder to carry it out, he may carry it out. If it is a tangible item ('ayn), it is stated in Ijarah al-Qunyah: if the tenant disappears after the year and does not hand the key to the other, the latter may make another key; and if he rents it out to someone else without the judge's permission, it is permissible. And a fatwa incident occurred: the period passed, the tenant disappeared and left his possessions in the house, so I gave the fatwa that he may open the house and live in it, and as for the possessions, he puts them aside until the owner returns, and opening does not require the judge's permission, taking from what is in al-Qunyah. And in Ghasb of Minyat al-Mufti: someone took the branches of a tree that project into the airspace of another's house, and the owner of the house cut the branches. If the branches are such that their owner can tie them with a rope and vacate the airspace, the cutter is liable; if it is not possible, he is not liable because if the matter were taken to the judge, he would order cutting from that place. If it is a debt, it is stated in Mudaayanat al-Qunyah: the creditor, if he finds from the debtor's wealth something of the same type as his right in the same condition, he may take it without the debtor's consent; he may not take a different type, like dirhams for dinars. According to al-Shafi'i, he may take to the value. According to Abu Bakr al-Razi, he may take dinars for dirhams and also dirhams for dinars by istihsan.
Source pages p. 328

Commentary
This passage compiles rulings from various Hanafi sources on when a person may enforce his own right without resorting to litigation (istifa' al-haq bi ghayr qada'). The author organizes by type of right: Hadd of slander (qadhf) – cannot be self-enforced because it involves Allah's right; retribution (qisas) – the guardian may execute the qisas by sword even without court order; ta'zir – both parties may be punished, starting with the aggressor; insult – the victim may say the like back, but it is better to avoid; husband's discipline of wife is permitted; tangible property – e.g., a tenant's disappearance allows the landlord to make a new key or re-rent without court permission; a neighbor may cut encroaching branches if they cannot be otherwise moved; debt – the creditor may take from the debtor's property of the same type without consent. Inter-madhhab differences are noted regarding taking non-like items.

Key Terms
استيفاء الحق (istīfā' al-ḥaqq): Taking or fulfilling one's right, here specifically by self-help without judicial proceeding.
حد القذف (ḥadd al-qadhf): The prescribed punishment for false accusation of unlawful intercourse.
قصاص (qiṣāṣ): Retributive justice, especially for intentional homicide or injury, where the victim's heir has the right to demand execution or forgiveness.
تعزير (taʿzīr): Discretionary punishment for offenses that do not have fixed penalties.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, can the wali (guardian) of a murder victim kill the murderer with a sword without a court verdict?
A) No, he must wait for a judge.
B) Yes, he may.
C) Yes, but only with a knife.
D) No, he must demand blood money instead.

Key Takeaway
Self-help is permitted for individual rights like qisas (with a sword) and ta'zir, but prohibited for hadd punishments that primarily involve Allah's right.

Self-Help in Property and Debt
Rulings on self-help in property and debt cases.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rulings on self-help for property and debt, including taking possession of collateral, dealing with encroaching branches, and recovering debts from the debtor's property.

Core Idea
A creditor may take property of the same type as his debt without consent, but cannot take a different type except by istihsan in some views.

In property and debt matters, Hanafi fiqh provides specific allowances for self-help to avoid prolonged litigation. This lesson covers cases where a person may take action without court permission, such as when a tenant abandons property, when branches encroach on airspace, and when a creditor finds assets of the debtor.

Guided Path
1
Identify the type of property right
Determine whether it involves a tangible asset (like a rented house) or a debt (like money owed).
2
Examine the conditions for self-help
For tenant disappearance: the landlord may make a new key or re-rent without judge's permission. For encroaching branches: if they cannot be moved, the neighbor may cut them. For debt: the creditor may take property of the same type and condition.
3
Note the limitation on type of property
The creditor may not take a different type (e.g., dirhams for dinars) except according to a minority view (Abu Bakr al-Razi) by istihsan.
4
Consider the inter-madhhab difference
Al-Shafi'i allows taking to the value, not restricted to the same type. Hanafi restricts to same type, but Abu Bakr al-Razi allows exchange by istihsan.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولم يذكر الشارحون هنا حكم استيفاء ذي الحق حقه من الغير بلا قضاء، وأحببت جمعه هنا من مواضعه تكثيراً للفوائد وتيسيراً على طالبيها فإن كان الحق حد قذف فلا يستوفيه بنفسه لأن فيه حق الله تعالى اتفاقاً، والأصح أن الغالب فيه حقه تعالى فلا يستوفيه إلا من يقيم الحدود ولكن بطلب المقذوف كما بيناه في بابه. وإن كان قصاصاً فقال في جنايات البزازية : قتل الرجل عمداً وله ولي له أن يقتص بالسيف قضى به أو لا، ويضرب علاوته ولو رام قتله بغير سيف منع، وإن فعل عزر لكن لا يضمن لاستيفائه حقه ا هـ . وإن كان تعزيراً ففي حدود القنية : ضرب غيره بغير حق وضربه المضروب أيضاً أنهما يعزران ويبدأ بإقامة التعزير بالبادئ منهما لأنه أظلم والوجوب عليه أسبق ا هـ . وأما إذا شتمه فله أن يقول له مثله والأولى تركه كما قدمناه في محله وقالوا للزوج أن يؤدب زوجته. وفي جامع الفصولين من التحليف : ومن عليه التعزير لو مكن صاحب الحق منه أقامه ا وإن كان عيناً ففي إجارة القنية : ولو غاب المستأجر بعد السنة ولم يسلم المفتاح إلى الآخر فله أن يتخذ له مفتاحاً آخر، ولو أجره من غيره بغير إذن الحاكم جاز ا هـ . وقد صارت حادثة الفتوى مضت المدة وغاب المستأجر وترك متاعه في الدار فأفتيت بأن له أن يفتح الدار ويسكن فيها، وأما المتاع فيجعله في ناحية إلى حضور صاحبه ولا يتوقف الفتح على إذن القاضي أخذاً مما في القنية وفي غصب منية المفتي : أخذت أغصان شجرة إنسان هواء دار آخر فقطع رب الدار الأغصان، فإن كانت الأغصان بحالة يمكن لصاحبها إن يشدها بحبل ويفرغ هواء داره ضمن القاطع، وإن لم يمكن لا يضمن إذا قطع من موضع لو رفع إلى الحاكم أمر بالقطع من ذلك الموضع ا هـ. وإن كان ديناً ففي مداينات القنية : رب الدين إذا ظفر من جنس حقه من مال المديون على صفته فله أخذه بغير رضاه ولا يأخذ خلاف جنسه كالدراهم والدنانير، وعند الشافعي له أخذه بقدر قيمته، وعن أبي بكر الرازي له أخذ الدنانير بالدراهم، وكذا أخذ الدراهم بالدنانير استحساناً

According to the Hanafi majority, can a creditor take a horse (different type) from the debtor to satisfy a debt of dirhams?

Defining Plaintiff and Defendant: Between Form and Reality
Examining various definitions of plaintiff and defendant with a focus on ma'na over sura.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi definitions of plaintiff and defendant, distinguish between form and meaning in legal classification, and apply the ma'na-over-sura principle to identify the true legal roles in a given dispute.

Core Idea
In Hanafi law, legal roles depend on underlying reality (ma'na), not mere form (sura).

The matn of Kanz al-Daqa'iq opens with a concise definition: the plaintiff is not compelled to litigate if he abandons, while the defendant is compelled. But this definition, while fundamental, is only one of several offered by Hanafi scholars. By parsing these definitions and studying the depositary example, you will grasp the master principle that reconciles them: the primacy of meaning over form.

Guided Path
1
State the matn definition
Read the opening line of the passage: 'al-mudda'i man idha taraka taraka wa al-mudda'a 'alayh bi-khilafih'. Understand that the defendant is compelled to respond, the plaintiff is not.
This is the basic, general definition relied upon in fatwa.
2
Explore alternative definitions
Identify three other definitions: (a) plaintiff needs proof, defendant's word suffices; (b) plaintiff seeks what is not apparent, defendant holds to the apparent; (c) Muhammad's definition: defendant is the denier.
These definitions capture different aspects of the litigation dynamic.
3
Analyze the depositary example
Consider the case of a depositary (mudi') who claims to have returned the deposit. In outward form, he is a plaintiff asserting a claim. But in underlying reality, he is denying liability, making him the defendant. This reveals the gap between form and meaning.
The example from al-Hidayah is the key to understanding the principle.
4
Extract the master principle
Conclude that the Hanafi school prioritizes meaning (ma'na) over form (sura). The classification of plaintiff/defendant is determined by the substantive legal issue, not the outward phrasing of the claim.
This principle governs the compulsion to litigate and the burden of proof.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: (المدعي من إذا ترك ترك والمدعى عليه بخلافه) أي المدعي من لا يجبر على الخصومة إذا تركها والمدعى عليه من يجبر على الخصومة إذا تركها ومعرفة الفرق بينهما من أهم ما يبتنى عليه مسائل الدعوى وقد اختلفت عبارات المشايخ فيه فمنها ما في الكتاب وهو حد عام صحيح، وقيل المدعي من لا يستحق إلا بحجة كالخارج، والمدعى عليه من يكون مستحقاً بقوله من غير حجة كذي اليد، وقيل المدعي من يلتمس غير الظاهر، والمدعى عليه من يتمسك بالظاهر. وقال محمد في الأصل: المدعى عليه هو المنكر وهذا صحيح لكن الشأن في معرفته والترجيح بالفقه عند الحذاق من أصحابنا لأن الاعتبار للمعاني دون الصور فإن المودع إذا قال رددت الوديعة فالقول قوله من اليمين وإن كان مدعياً للرد صورة لأنه ينكر الضمان؛ كذا في الهداية وحاصله أن المدعي يدعي فراغ ذمته عن الضمان ولهذا تقبل بينته اعتباراً للصورة ويجبر على الخصومة ويحلف اعتباراً للمعنى؛ كذا في الكافي. وفي المجتبى : الصحيح ما في الكتاب والمراد أن المدعى عليه يجبر على أصل الخصومة، ولا ينافيه قول محمد إن الخيار للمدعى عليه في تعيين القاضي كما لا يخفى. وفي الخانية : ولو كان في البلدة قاضيان كل واحد منهما في محلة على حدة فوقعت الخصومة بين رجلين أحدهما من
His saying: (The plaintiff is the one who, if he abandons, he leaves; and the defendant is contrary to that) i.e., the plaintiff is the one who is not compelled to litigation if he abandons it, and the defendant is the one who is compelled to litigation if he abandons it. Knowing the difference between them is among the most important things on which the issues of claims are based. The expressions of the scholars differ therein. Among them is what is in the book (the matn), which is a general and correct definition. It is also said: The plaintiff is the one who does not deserve anything except through proof, like the outsider (claimant); and the defendant is the one who deserves by his word without proof, like the possessor. It is also said: The plaintiff is the one who seeks what is not apparent; and the defendant is the one who holds on to the apparent. Muhammad said in al-Asl: The defendant is the one who denies. This is correct, but the issue is identifying him, and the preference is by fiqh among the perceptive of our companions because consideration is given to meanings, not forms. For if the depositary says, 'I returned the deposit,' his word is accepted with an oath, although in form he is a plaintiff claiming return, because he denies liability. So in al-Hidaya. The summary is that the plaintiff claims the discharge of his liability; therefore his evidence is accepted considering the form, and he is compelled to litigation and takes an oath considering the meaning. So in al-Kafi. In al-Mujtaba: The correct is what is in the book, and the meaning is that the defendant is compelled to the very lawsuit; and this is not contradicted by Muhammad's statement that the defendant has the choice of selecting the judge, as is not hidden. In al-Khaniyya: If there are two judges in the town, each in a separate district, and a dispute occurs between two men, one of whom...
Source pages p. 329

Commentary
This passage delves into the definitions of plaintiff (mudda'i) and defendant (mudda'a 'alayh) as they appear in the matn of Kanz al-Daqa'iq. The commentator explains the matn's definition: the plaintiff is not forced to continue if he drops the case, while the defendant is forced. Alternative definitions are given: one based on burden of proof (plaintiff needs proof, defendant's word is taken on oath due to possession), another based on deviation from the apparent state (plaintiff seeks what is contrary to appearance, defendant supports the status quo). Imam Muhammad's definition (defendant is the denier) is also endorsed, but the commentator emphasizes that reality (ma'na) takes precedence over form (sura). The example of a depositary claiming return illustrates: in form he is plaintiff, but in meaning he is defendant (denying liability), so he is not forced to prove return but takes an oath. The passage also notes that despite being compelled to participate, the defendant still has the right to choose the judge per Muhammad. The text ends mid-sentence discussing a scenario with two judges.

Key Terms
مدعي (mudda'i): Plaintiff; the one who brings a claim. Hanafi: defined primarily by not being compelled if he drops the case; also by need for proof or seeking non-apparent, but ma'na determines true role.
مدعى عليه (mudda'a 'alayh): Defendant; the one against whom claim is made. Hanafi: compelled to respond; may be identified as denier or holder of the apparent.
خصومة (khusuma): Litigation, lawsuit.
بينة (bayyina): Evidence, proof (usually two witnesses).
يمين (yamin): Oath; the defendant may be required to swear.
معنى (ma'na): Meaning, underlying reality (as opposed to form).
صورة (sura): Form, outward appearance.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the example of the depositary (mudi') claiming he returned the deposit, who is considered the defendant in meaning?
A) The depositor (owner) who claims the deposit.
B) The depositary who claims to have returned it.
C) Both are defendants.
D) Neither, they are both plaintiffs.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi classification of plaintiff and defendant is based on underlying legal reality (ma'na), not mere form (sura), affecting compulsion to litigate and burden of proof.

Conditions for Describing a Claimed Object
States the conditions for valid description of a claimed object

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions for describing a claimed object in Hanafi fiqh and apply them to scenarios involving measurable goods, salam, and currency.

Core Idea
If the claimed object is not present, its valid description is wajib for the claim's acceptance.

In Hanafi court procedure, when the object of a claim cannot be brought before the judge, the claimant must describe it in sufficient detail. This passage from Kanz al-Daqaiq, as explained in al-Bahr al-Raiq, specifies the necessary elements of that description for different types of property. Mastering these conditions ensures that a claim is legally sound.

Guided Path
1
Identify the general rule
The claimant must produce the object; if not, he must describe it. This is wajib.
2
Detail the description for measurable items (makilat)
Mention genus, type, description, and quantity.
e.g., 'Egyptian red wheat, grade A, 100 kg.'
3
Detail the description for salam claims
State the conditions of the salam contract, including capital genus, type, description, quantity by weight if applicable, and payment at session.
4
Apply the simplified formula for contracts with many conditions
Saying 'due to a valid such-and-such' suffices without enumerating each condition.
e.g., 'due to a valid sale' is acceptable.
5
Detail the description for currency (gold/silver)
Specify genus, type (minted origin), and quality (good/medium/bad) if multiple currencies exist.
e.g., 'Bukhari minting, fine quality.'

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كلف إحضارها ليشير إليها بالدعوى وكذا في الشهادات والاستحلاف فإن تعذر ذكر الأوصاف. ولا بد من ذكر النوع والوصف مع ذكر الجنس والقدر في المكيلات، ويذكر في السلم شرائطه من إعلام جنس رأس المال وغيره ونوعه وصفته وقدره بالوزن إن كان وزنياً وانتقاده بالمجلس حتى يصح، ولو قال بسبب بيع صحيح جرى بينهما صحت الدعوى بلا خلاف، وعلى هذا في كل سبب له شرائط كثيرة يكتفى بقوله بسبب كذا صحيح. وإن ادعى ذهباً أو فضة فلا بد من بيان جنسه ونوعه إن كان مضروباً كبخاري الضرب وصفته جيد أو وسط أو رديء إذا كان في البلد نقود مختلفة. وفي العمادي: إذا كان في البلد نقود وأحدها أروج لا تصح الدعوى ما لم يبين وتمامه في البزازية وخزانة المفتين.
He is required to bring it to point to it in the claim, and likewise in testimonies and swearing. If it is impossible, then mention the description. It is necessary to mention the type and description along with mention of the genus and quantity in measurable items. In salam, its conditions are mentioned: informing of the genus of capital, etc., its type, description, quantity by weight if it is weighed, and its payment in the session for it to be valid. If he says 'due to a valid sale that occurred between them,' the claim is valid without dispute. And accordingly, for every cause that has many conditions, it suffices to say 'due to a valid such-and-such.' If he claims gold or silver, it is necessary to specify its genus and type if it is minted, like Bukhari minting, and its quality: good, medium, or bad, if in the country there are different currencies. In al-Imadi: if in the country there are currencies and one of them is more prevalent, the claim is not valid unless he specifies. Its completion is in al-Bazzaziyya and Khizanat al-Muftiyin.
Source pages p. 334

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement that lays out the requirements for describing the object claimed in a lawsuit. The general rule is that the claimant must bring the object if possible; if not, he must describe it. For measurable items (makilat), he must declare the genus, type, description, and quantity. For salam (forward sale) claims, the conditions of the salam contract must be stated. A simplified formula 'due to a valid contract' is sufficient for contracts with many conditions. For currency claims, the specific mint and quality must be given if multiple currencies circulate. This ruling is from the Hanafi school as stated in the authoritative sources.

Key Terms
المدعى به (al-mudda'a bihi): The object that is claimed in the lawsuit
المكيلات (al-makilat): Items measured by volume, like grains
السلم (al-salam): Forward sale; a sale with deferred delivery of goods and immediate payment

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a claimant says 'due to a valid sale' without detailing the conditions, is the claim valid according to the Hanafi school?
A) No, it is invalid.
B) Yes, it is valid.
C) It depends on the judge's discretion.
D) Only if the sale was documented.

Key Takeaway
The description of a claimed object must include specific details according to its type; if not produced, the description is wajib and omission invalidates the claim.

Bringing the Claimed Object to Court: Requirement and Exceptions
Explains the requirement to bring the claimed object and exceptions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling on bringing movable property to court and identify when the defendant is exempt due to heavy expense or deposit.

Core Idea
The defendant must bring light movable items to court; heavy items and deposits excepted.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq expands on the requirement for the defendant to bring the claimed object to court. The Hanafi school holds it wajib for movable property, but the practical application has important exceptions: if transport is costly or the object is a deposit, the duty changes. A key dispute between Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn over a stolen cow illustrates that the object's presence is not a condition for the validity of the claim itself.

Guided Path
1
Identify the general ruling
The text begins with the ruling: if the object is in the defendant's hand, he is required to bring it to court for pointing in the claim, testimony, and swearing.
This is the default ruling for movable property.
2
Locate the exception for heavy items
The ruling is restricted to what has no carriage or expense (haml wa mu'na). The defendant is not compelled to bring heavy items.
Various definitions of 'heavy' are given: cannot be carried with one hand, requires paid transport, or price varies by locale.
3
Analyze the cow case dispute
Witnesses testify to a stolen cow but differ on its color. Abu Hanifa accepts the testimony, the Sahibayn reject it. This shows that bringing the object is not a condition for claim validity.
The commentator notes that the absence of the object is not problematic; value can suffice.
4
Distinguish the deposit case
If the defendant is a trustee (muda'), he is only required to vacate (takhliyah), not to bring the object to court.
The duty changes because the trustee is not the owner.
5
Note the procedural requirement
The claimant must explicitly say 'It is obligatory upon him to bring it to court for me to establish evidence' to compel the defendant. If the defendant admits, no compulsion arises.
This protects the defendant if he is willing to admit ownership.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (وإن كان عيناً في يد المدعى عليه كلف إحضارها) ليشير إليها بالدعوى وكذا في الشهادات والاستحلاف لأن الإعلام بأقصى ما يمكن شرط وذلك بالإشارة في المنقول لأن النقل ممكن والإشارة أبلغ في التعريف حتى قالوا في المنقولات التي يتعذر نقلها كالرحى ونحوه حضر الحاكم عندها أو بعث أميناً، وفي المجتبى معزواً في مسألة الشاهدين : إذا شهدوا على سرقة بقرة واختلفا في لونها تقبل الشهادة خلافاً لهما وهذه المسألة تدل على أن إحضار المنقول ليس بشرط لصحة الدعوى، ولو شرط لأحضرت ولما وقع الاختلاف عند المشاهدة في لونها ثم قال : وهذه المسألة الناس عنها غافلون ا.هـ. قلت: لا تدل لأنها إذا كانت غائبة لا يشترط إحضارها والقيمة كافية كما سيأتي فليتأمل وفي جامع الفصولين : وفي دعوى إحضار المدعي مجلس الحكم لا بد أن يقول فواجب عليه إحضاره مجلس الحكم لأقيم البينة عليه إن كان جاحداً، ولا بد من ذكر هذه اللفظة في الدعوى لأن ذا اليد لو كان مقراً لا يلزم الإحضار لأنه يأخذ من المقر والأمر بالإحضار إنما يصح لو منكراً، أما لو كان مودعاً عنده لا الأمر بإحضاره إذ الواجب فيه التخلية لا نقلها، فلو أنكر ذو اليد يصح الإحضار يكون محقاً. ادعى عيناً في يده وأراد إحضاره مجلس الحكم فأنكر المدعى عليه كونه في يده فبرهن المدعي أنه كان بيد المدعى عليه قبل هذا التاريخ بسنة، هل يقبل ويجبر المدعى عليه على إحضاره بهذه البيئة أم لا؟ كانت واقعة الفتوى وينبغي أن تقبل إذا ثبت في يده في الزمان الماضي ولم يثبت خروجه من يده فتبقى ولا تزول بشك ا هـ . أطلق في لزوم إحضارها وهو مقيد بما لا حمل له ولا مؤنة أما ماله حمل ومؤنة فإن المدعى عليه لا يجبر على إحضاره، وتفسير الحمل والمؤنة بحال يحمل إلى مجلس القاضي بأجر لا مجاناً فهذا مما له حمل ومؤنة، وذكر بعده بورقتين أن ما لا يمكن حمله بيد واحدة فهو مما له حمل ومؤنة . وقيل : ما يحتاج في نقله إلى مؤنة كبر وشعير فهو مما له حمل ومؤنة لا ما لا يحتاج في نقله إلى المؤنة كمسك وزعفران قليل . وقيل : ما اختلف سعره في البلدان فهو مما له حمل ومؤنة لا ما اتفق اهـ . ثم ذكر فيه مسائل فيما إذا وصف المدعي المدعى فلما حضر خالف في البعض، وحاصله أنه إن ترك الدعوى الأولى وادعى الحاضر تسمع لأنها مبتدأة وإلا فلا. وبما قررناه علم أن في كلام المصنف وغيره تساهلاً إذ في دعوى عين وديعة لا يكلف إحضارها إنما يكلف التخلية.
His statement: (If it is an object in the hand of the defendant, he is required to bring it) to point to it in the claim, and likewise in testimonies and swearing, because informing to the utmost possible is a condition, and that is by pointing in movable property because transport is possible and pointing is more definitive. They said regarding movable properties that are difficult to transport, like a millstone, etc., the judge goes to it or sends a trustee. In al-Mujtaba, attributed in the issue of two witnesses: if they testify about the theft of a cow and differ on its color, the testimony is accepted, contrary to the two imams. This issue indicates that bringing movable property is not a condition for the validity of the claim; if it were a condition, it would have been brought, and there would be no disagreement upon viewing its color. Then he said: people are heedless of this issue. I say: it does not indicate because if it is absent, bringing it is not required and its value is sufficient, as will come, so contemplate. In Jami' al-Fusulayn: In the claim of bringing the claimant to the court, he must say 'It is obligatory upon him to bring it to the court for me to establish the evidence against him if he denies.' This phrase must be mentioned in the claim, because if the possessor admits, bringing is not incumbent since he takes from the admitter; the order to bring is only valid if he denies. But if it is a deposit with him, there is no order to bring it because the obligation is to vacate, not to transport. So if the possessor denies, the bringing is valid and he is correct. If someone claims an object in his hand and wants to bring it to court, and the defendant denies it was in his hand, and the claimant proves that it was in the defendant's hand a year before, is it accepted and is the defendant compelled to bring it by this evidence? This was a fatwa case, and it should be accepted if its being in his hand in the past is established and its leaving his hand is not established, so it remains and does not go away by doubt. The absolute ruling on the necessity of bringing it is restricted to what has no carriage and no expense; as for what has carriage and expense, the defendant is not compelled to bring it. The interpretation of carriage and expense is that it is transported to the judge's court for a fee, not for free; that is what has carriage and expense. It was mentioned two pages later that what cannot be carried with one hand is what has carriage and expense. Some say: what requires expense in transport like wheat and barley is what has carriage and expense, not what does not require expense like musk and a little saffron. Some say: what differs in price between countries is what has carriage and expense, not what is the same. Then he mentioned issues regarding when the claimant describes the claimed item, and when it is brought, it differs in some respects. The gist is that if he abandons the first claim and claims the present one, it is heard because it is a new claim; otherwise not. From what we have established, it is known that there is leniency in the words of the author and others, because in a claim of a deposited object, he is not required to bring it; he is only required to vacate.
Source pages p. 334

Commentary
This passage is an expansion probe on the ruling regarding bringing the claimed object to court. The author begins by explaining the wisdom: pointing to the object is the most definitive identification for movable property. However, if transport is difficult (like a millstone), the judge goes to it or sends a trustee. A key proof is the hadith case of two witnesses testifying to a stolen cow but differing on its color: the testimony is accepted per Abu Hanifa (contrary to the Sahibayn), showing that bringing the object is not a condition for validity of the claim. The commentator rebuts that this only applies when the object is absent; value can suffice. The text then details the precise wording required in the claim to compel the defendant to bring the object, distinguishing between an admittor and a denier. There is also a fatwa case on proving past possession. The ruling is restricted to light items; heavy items (with carriage and expense) exempt the defendant from bringing. Several definitions of 'heavy' are given. Finally, if the brought object differs from the description, the claimant may start a new claim. A key takeaway: for deposits, the trustee is only required to vacate, not bring.

Key Terms
المنقول (al-manqul): Movable property that can be transported
الحمل والمؤنة (al-haml wa al-mu'na): Carriage and expense; the cost of transporting the object

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the ruling if the defendant is a trustee (mudi') of a deposited object and is sued for its return?
A) He must bring the object to court.
B) He is only required to vacate, not bring it.
C) He must pay its value.
D) He must bring it only if the judge orders.

Key Takeaway
The defendant's duty to bring the claimed object is limited to light movable items; heavy items and deposits have special exceptions.
