Specifying Value When the Object is Absent
Explains the requirement to state the value if the object is destroyed or absent

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on specifying value when the claimed object is destroyed or absent, and identify the exceptions for difficult-to-keep items and animals.

Core Idea
When the claimed object cannot be produced, its value must be specified – but for difficulty-preserved items, only direct inspection suffices.

This lesson from Al-Bahr al-Raiq explains a key procedural rule in court claims: if the object itself is gone, the claimant must state its value. However, not all absent objects are treated alike – items like millstones or large heaps of food require the judge to send a trustee for inspection, and for animals the judge has a choice to go himself or send a representative.

Guided Path
1
Identify the primary ruling
When the claimed object is destroyed or absent, the claimant must mention its value to make the claim known.
2
Understand the reason
The object cannot be known by description alone; value is knowable through description, while sight is impossible.
This is the 'illa (reason) for the ruling.
3
Apply the exception for difficult-to-keep items
For items like millstones, heaps of food, and similar things that are hard to preserve, the judge sends a trustee; the value alone is not sufficient.
These items require direct inspection despite absence.
4
Handle animals
For a claimed animal, the judge may either go out to see it or send someone to hear the claim and testimony in its presence.
The judge has discretion.
5
Note the implication for multiple items
If a claimant claims various objects of different types and mentions only a lump sum value, the value must be broken down per item.
General lump sum is insufficient.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (فإن تعذر ذكر قيمتها) أي بهلاكها أو غيبتها فلا بد من ذكر قيمتها ليصير المدعى به معلوماً لأن العين لا تعرف بالوصف والقيمة تعرف به وقد تعذر مشاهدة العين. وإنما قيدنا التعذر بالهلاك أو الغيبة لئلا يرد الرحى وصبرة الطعام ونحو ذلك مما يتعذر بقائه فإن القاضي يبعث أمينه كما قدمناه ولا يكتفي بذكر القيمة. وفي الدابة يخير القاضي إن شاء خرج وإن شاء بعث إليها من يسمع الدعوى والشهادة بحضرتها كما في جامع الفصولين وفيه : ادعى أعياناً مختلفة الجنس والنوع والصفة وذكر قيمة الكل جملة ولم يبين. قوله : (وينبغي أن تقبل إذا ثبت في يده الخ) قال في نور العين يقول الحقير: الظاهر أن قوله «ينبغي» لا. القاضي فشهدوا بأنه له ولم يشهدوا بأنه ملكه يجوز لأن اللام للتمليك، وكذلك إن شهدوا أن هذا مالك له أو شهدوا على إقرار المدعى عليه بأنه للمدعي وذلك لا إشكال فيه، إنما الإشكال فيما لو ادعى أنه أقر بهذا الشيء ولم يدع بأنه ملكي وأقام الشهود على ذلك هل يقبل. وهل يقضي بالملك؟ منهم من يقول نعم فقد ذكرنا أن الشهود لو شهدوا بأن هذا أقر بهذا الشيء له تقبل وإن لم يشهدوا بأنه ملكه وكذلك المدعي وأكثرهم على أنه لا تصح الدعوى ما لم يقل أقر به وهو ملكي لأن الإقرار خبر والخبر يحتمل الصدق والكذب، فإذا كان كذباً لا يوجب والمدعي يقول أقر به لي يصير بالملك والإقرار غير موجب له فلم توجد دعوى الملك فلهذا شرط قوله وهو ملكي بخلاف الشهادة لأن الثابت بها كالثابت بالمعاينة .اهـ. ملخصاً. قوله : (إذا كانت غائبة الأظهر أن يقول هالكة. قوله : (وينبغي أن تقبل إذا ثبت في يده الخ) قال في نور العين يقول الحقير: الظاهر أن قوله «ينبغي» لا مدعياً.
His statement: (If it is impossible, mention its value) that is, due to its destruction or absence, so it is necessary to mention its value so that the claimed item becomes known, because the object is not known by description while value is known by it, and viewing the object is impossible. We restricted impossibility to destruction or absence so that it does not apply to a millstone, a heap of food, etc., which are difficult to keep; the judge sends his trustee as we mentioned, and he is not satisfied with mentioning the value. Regarding an animal, the judge may choose: if he wishes, he goes out, or if he wishes, he sends someone to hear the claim and testimony in its presence, as in Jami' al-Fusulayn. In it: he claimed various objects of different genus, type, and description and mentioned the total value as a lump sum and did not specify. His statement: (It should be accepted if it is established in his hand, etc.) He said in Nur al-Ayn: the humble one says: it appears that his word 'should' does not. The judge: they testified that it is for him but did not testify that it is his property; it is permissible because 'lam' indicates ownership. Similarly, if they testified that he is its owner, or they testified about the defendant's admission that it belongs to the claimant, there is no problem. The problem is if he claimed that he admitted this thing and did not claim it as his property, and he brought witnesses on that, is it accepted? Does the judge rule on ownership? Some say yes, we mentioned that if witnesses testify that he admitted this thing, it is accepted even if they did not testify that it is his property, and similarly the claimant. But most say that the claim is not valid unless he says 'he admitted and it is my property,' because admission is a report, and a report may be true or false. If it is false, it does not oblige, and the claimant says 'he admitted to me to become ownership,' but admission is not a cause of ownership, so no claim of ownership exists. Hence the condition of saying 'it is my property' is required, unlike testimony because what is established by testimony is like what is established by direct observation. Summary. His statement: (If it is absent, the most apparent is to say destroyed. His statement: (It should be accepted if it is established in his hand, etc.) He said in Nur al-Ayn: the humble one says: it appears that his word 'should' is not claimant.
Source pages p. 335

Commentary
This passage discusses the situation where the claimed object is impossible to present due to destruction or absence. The author clarifies that in such cases, the value must be specified, because the object itself cannot be described adequately without sight. However, for items that are difficult to keep, like a millstone or large heap of food, the judge must send a trustee rather than accept the value. For animals, the judge may go himself or send a representative. If multiple objects are claimed together, the value must be broken down per item. The passage then shifts to a discussion on testimony: if witnesses testify that an object 'is for him' (lahu) without stating 'it is his property' (milk), it is accepted because 'lam' implies ownership. A dispute arises: if a claimant says 'the defendant admitted to me' without claiming ownership, is the claim valid? Most Hanafis require the claimant to explicitly say 'it is my property' because admission alone does not establish ownership. This differs from testimony where the fact of admission is sufficient. The final parts appear fragmented but touch on the distinction between 'absent' and 'destroyed'.

Key Terms
القيمة (al-qima): The monetary value of the claimed object
التمليك (al-tamlik): The legal transfer of ownership

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a claimant claims a destroyed object, what must he specify according to the Hanafi school?
A) Only the object's description.
B) Only its value.
C) Both description and value.
D) Neither; the claim is invalid.

Key Takeaway
The value of a destroyed or absent claimed object must be specified to make the claim definite, with exceptions for difficult-to-keep items that require trustee inspection.

Requirement of Explicit Ownership in Claim Based on Admission
Discusses whether a claimant must say 'it is my property' when claiming based on admission

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi majority view that a claimant must explicitly state ownership (milk) when basing a claim on the defendant's admission, and distinguish this from testimony.

Core Idea
A claim based solely on an admission does not establish ownership unless the claimant adds 'it is my property'.

This part of the passage from Al-Bahr al-Raiq addresses a subtle but important point in court procedure: what wording is necessary for a valid claim of ownership when relying on the other party's admission. The author reports a disagreement among Hanafis on whether the claimant must explicitly say 'it is my property' (milk) or if merely stating 'he admitted this to me' suffices. The majority view requires the explicit ownership statement, and this lesson unpacks the reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the issue
The claimant says: 'The defendant admitted this thing to me' but does not add 'it is my property'. Is the claim valid?
2
Compare with testimony
If witnesses testify that the object is for the claimant (lahu), it is accepted without explicit ownership because 'lam' indicates ownership. Similarly, testimony on admission is sufficient without extra wording.
Testimony establishes fact, unlike a claim.
3
Understand the reason for the requirement
Admission is a report (khabar) that can be true or false. It does not cause ownership. So the claimant must assert ownership to make the claim one of ownership.
4
Identify the preferred view
Most Hanafi scholars say the claim is not valid unless the claimant says 'he admitted and it is my property'.
This is the mufta bihi position.
5
Note the practical consequence
If the claimant fails to say 'it is my property', the claim may be dismissed, or the judge may ask for clarification.

قوله : (فإن تعذر ذكر قيمتها) أي بهلاكها أو غيبتها فلا بد من ذكر قيمتها ليصير المدعى به معلوماً لأن العين لا تعرف بالوصف والقيمة تعرف به وقد تعذر مشاهدة العين. وإنما قيدنا التعذر بالهلاك أو الغيبة لئلا يرد الرحى وصبرة الطعام ونحو ذلك مما يتعذر بقائه فإن القاضي يبعث أمينه كما قدمناه ولا يكتفي بذكر القيمة. وفي الدابة يخير القاضي إن شاء خرج وإن شاء بعث إليها من يسمع الدعوى والشهادة بحضرتها كما في جامع الفصولين وفيه : ادعى أعياناً مختلفة الجنس والنوع والصفة وذكر قيمة الكل جملة ولم يبين. قوله : (وينبغي أن تقبل إذا ثبت في يده الخ) قال في نور العين يقول الحقير: الظاهر أن قوله «ينبغي» لا. القاضي فشهدوا بأنه له ولم يشهدوا بأنه ملكه يجوز لأن اللام للتمليك، وكذلك إن شهدوا أن هذا مالك له أو شهدوا على إقرار المدعى عليه بأنه للمدعي وذلك لا إشكال فيه، إنما الإشكال فيما لو ادعى أنه أقر بهذا الشيء ولم يدع بأنه ملكي وأقام الشهود على ذلك هل يقبل. وهل يقضي بالملك؟ منهم من يقول نعم فقد ذكرنا أن الشهود لو شهدوا بأن هذا أقر بهذا الشيء له تقبل وإن لم يشهدوا بأنه ملكه وكذلك المدعي وأكثرهم على أنه لا تصح الدعوى ما لم يقل أقر به وهو ملكي لأن الإقرار خبر والخبر يحتمل الصدق والكذب، فإذا كان كذباً لا يوجب والمدعي يقول أقر به لي يصير بالملك والإقرار غير موجب له فلم توجد دعوى الملك فلهذا شرط قوله وهو ملكي بخلاف الشهادة لأن الثابت بها كالثابت بالمعاينة .اهـ. ملخصاً. قوله : (إذا كانت غائبة الأظهر أن يقول هالكة. قوله : (وينبغي أن تقبل إذا ثبت في يده الخ) قال في نور العين يقول الحقير: الظاهر أن قوله «ينبغي» لا مدعياً.
His statement: (If it is impossible, mention its value) that is, due to its destruction or absence, so it is necessary to mention its value so that the claimed item becomes known, because the object is not known by description while value is known by it, and viewing the object is impossible. We restricted impossibility to destruction or absence so that it does not apply to a millstone, a heap of food, etc., which are difficult to keep; the judge sends his trustee as we mentioned, and he is not satisfied with mentioning the value. Regarding an animal, the judge may choose: if he wishes, he goes out, or if he wishes, he sends someone to hear the claim and testimony in its presence, as in Jami' al-Fusulayn. In it: he claimed various objects of different genus, type, and description and mentioned the total value as a lump sum and did not specify. His statement: (It should be accepted if it is established in his hand, etc.) He said in Nur al-Ayn: the humble one says: it appears that his word 'should' does not. The judge: they testified that it is for him but did not testify that it is his property; it is permissible because 'lam' indicates ownership. Similarly, if they testified that he is its owner, or they testified about the defendant's admission that it belongs to the claimant, there is no problem. The problem is if he claimed that he admitted this thing and did not claim it as his property, and he brought witnesses on that, is it accepted? Does the judge rule on ownership? Some say yes, we mentioned that if witnesses testify that he admitted this thing, it is accepted even if they did not testify that it is his property, and similarly the claimant. But most say that the claim is not valid unless he says 'he admitted and it is my property,' because admission is a report, and a report may be true or false. If it is false, it does not oblige, and the claimant says 'he admitted to me to become ownership,' but admission is not a cause of ownership, so no claim of ownership exists. Hence the condition of saying 'it is my property' is required, unlike testimony because what is established by testimony is like what is established by direct observation. Summary. His statement: (If it is absent, the most apparent is to say destroyed. His statement: (It should be accepted if it is established in his hand, etc.) He said in Nur al-Ayn: the humble one says: it appears that his word 'should' is not claimant.
Source pages p. 335

Commentary
This passage discusses the situation where the claimed object is impossible to present due to destruction or absence. The author clarifies that in such cases, the value must be specified, because the object itself cannot be described adequately without sight. However, for items that are difficult to keep, like a millstone or large heap of food, the judge must send a trustee rather than accept the value. For animals, the judge may go himself or send a representative. If multiple objects are claimed together, the value must be broken down per item. The passage then shifts to a discussion on testimony: if witnesses testify that an object 'is for him' (lahu) without stating 'it is his property' (milk), it is accepted because 'lam' implies ownership. A dispute arises: if a claimant says 'the defendant admitted to me' without claiming ownership, is the claim valid? Most Hanafis require the claimant to explicitly say 'it is my property' because admission alone does not establish ownership. This differs from testimony where the fact of admission is sufficient. The final parts appear fragmented but touch on the distinction between 'absent' and 'destroyed'.

Key Terms
القيمة (al-qima): The monetary value of the claimed object
التمليك (al-tamlik): The legal transfer of ownership

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the majority of Hanafi scholars, what additional phrase must a claimant include when claiming based on the defendant's admission?
A) 'He admitted to me'.
B) 'It is my property'.
C) 'I am the owner'.
D) No additional phrase is needed.

Key Takeaway
When claiming ownership based on the defendant's admission, the claimant must explicitly say 'it is my property' to make a valid claim of ownership.

Value Specification in Claims for Absent Property
Ruling with proof on value specification

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rule on when value must be specified in a property claim and identify the exceptions for ghasb and rahn.

Core Idea
Value is only required when the item cannot be produced; in ghasb and rahn it is never required.

When a claimant sues for an absent item, must they state its value? The Hanafi school makes a nuanced distinction: value specification is a procedural condition that only kicks in once it becomes clear the item cannot be produced in court. Even then, two major exceptions apply – claims for usurpation (ghasb) and pledge (rahn). This lesson unpacks these rules and the reasoning behind them.

Guided Path
1
Identify the general rule
The passage states that the condition of mentioning value arises only when it becomes impossible to produce the claimed item (ta'addhur ihdar al-'ayn). If the item is present, pointing suffices.
This is a procedural condition, not a substantive requirement.
2
Distinguish theft claims
In theft claims, value is required to determine whether the nisab (minimum threshold) for theft punishment is met.
This is a special rule for theft, not applicable to other claims.
3
Analyze the exceptions for ghasb and rahn
For claims of usurpation (ghasb) and pledge (rahn), the general rule requiring value does not apply even if the item is absent. The majority of Hanafi scholars accept the claim without value specification.
The rationale is that the claimant may not know the value, and the defendant can be coerced through imprisonment.
4
Understand the consequence of the exception
When value is not required, the word regarding the amount of value lies with the defendant (the usurper or pledgee). If the item cannot be produced, the defendant is imprisoned until he brings it; if impossible, judgment is passed for the value as per the defendant's statement.
This balances the claimant's ignorance with the need to proceed.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يعرف يذكر قيمة كل عين على حدة اختلف فيه المشايخ فقيل لا بد من بيان التفصيل، وقيل يكتفى بالإجمال وهو الصحيح ا هـ . وفي السراجية : ادعى عبيداً يبين جنسهم وسنهم و وصفتهم وحليتهم وقيمتهم وإن كان المدعي حاضراً كفت الإشارة، وظاهر كلام المصنف وغيره أن اشتراط ذكر القيمة إنما هو عند تعذر إحضار العين أما قبل ظهور التعذر فلا قال في الخانية : إنما يشترط ذكر القيمة في الدعوى إذا كانت دعوى سرقة ليعلم أنها نصاب أو لا، فأما فيما سوى ذلك فلا حاجة إلى بيانها ا هـ . وأطلق في وجوب بيان القيمة عند التعذر واستثنوا منه دعوى الغصب والرهن ففي جامع الفصولين: لو ادعى عيناً غائباً لا مكانه بأن ادعى أنه غصب منه ثوباً أو قناً ولا يدري قيامه وهلاكه فلو بين الجنس والصفة والقيمة تقبل دعواه، ولو لم يبين قيمته أشار في عامة الكتب إلى أنها تقبل فإنه ذكر في كتاب الرهن : لو ادعى أنه رهن عنده ثوباً وهو ينكر تسمع دعواه. وذكر في كتاب الغصب : ادعى أنه غصب منه أمة وبرهن تسمع وبعض مشايخنا قالوا : إنما تسمع دعواه إذا ذكر القيمة. وهذا تأويل ما ذكر في الكتاب أن الشهود شهدوا على إقرار المدعى عليه بالغصب فيثبت غصب القن بإقراره في حق الحبس والحكم جميعاً وعامة المشايخ على أن هذه الدعوى والبينة تقبل ولكن في حق الحبس وإطلاق محمد في الكتاب يدل عليه ومعنى الحبس أن يحبسه حتى يحضره ليعيد البينة على عينه فلو قال لا أقدر عليه حبس قدر ما لو قدر أحضره ثم يقضى عليه بقيمته ا هـ . والحاصل أنه في دعوى الغصب والرهن لا يشترط بيان الجنس والقيمة في صحة الدعوى والشهادة ويكون القول في القيمة للغاصب والمرتهن.
He knows: he mentions the value of each item separately. The mashayikh differed: it is said that detailing is necessary, and it is said that summary is sufficient, and that is the correct view. And in al-Sirajiyyah: If he claims slaves, he must specify their type, age, description, appearance, and value; and if the claimant is present, pointing is sufficient. And the apparent meaning of the author and others is that the condition of mentioning value is only when it is impossible to produce the item; before impossibility appears, it is not required. He said in al-Khaniyyah: Mentioning value in a claim is only required if it is a theft claim to know whether it reaches the nisab or not; otherwise, there is no need to mention it. And he made absolute the obligation of mentioning value when impossibility appears, and they excluded from it claims of ghasb and rahn. In Jami' al-Fusulayn: If he claims an absent item, e.g., he claims that a garment or slave was usurped from him and he does not know its existence or destruction, if he mentions type, description, and value, his claim is accepted; and if he does not mention its value, the general books indicate it is accepted, for it is mentioned in the Book of Rahn: if he claims that a garment was pledged with him and the other denies, his claim is heard. And in the Book of Ghasb: he claimed that a slave-girl was usurped from him and produced evidence; it is heard. Some of our mashayikh said: His claim is only heard if he mentions the value. This is an interpretation of what is mentioned in the book that the witnesses testified to the defendant's confession of ghasb, so the ghasb of the slave is established by his confession regarding both imprisonment and judgment. The general mashayikh hold that this claim and evidence are accepted but regarding imprisonment. And the absolute statement of Muhammad in the book indicates that. The meaning of imprisonment is that he is imprisoned until he brings it to re-establish evidence on the item; if he says 'I cannot,' he is imprisoned as much as would enable him to bring it, then judgment is passed against him for its value. The conclusion: in claims of ghasb and rahn, specification of type and value is not required for the validity of the claim and testimony, and the word regarding value is with the usurper or pledgee.
Source pages p. 336

Commentary
This passage is a ruling on the requirement to specify value in a claim for an absent item. The author of al-Hidayah comments on the matn stating that value must be mentioned. The mashayikh differed: some required detailed specification, but the correct view is that a summary is sufficient. According to al-Sirajiyyah, if the claimed item is present, pointing suffices; but for absent items, the claimant must describe type, age, appearance, and value. The commentator clarifies that the condition of mentioning value only applies when it becomes impossible to produce the item (ta'addhur al-ihdar). Before that, it is not required. An exception is made for theft claims where value is needed to check nisab. For ghasb and rahn claims, even when the item is absent, mentioning value is not required for admissibility; the claim is heard without it, as indicated in al-Khaniyyah and Jami' al-Fusulayn. The majority of scholars hold that such claims are accepted, but the defendant may be imprisoned until he produces the item. If he cannot, the court assesses its value. The principle is that in ghasb and rahn, the claimant may not know the value, so the burden of proof is relaxed. The word regarding the amount of value is with the usurper or pledgee (the defendant).

Key Terms
تعذر إحضار العين (ta'addhur ihdar al-'ayn): Impossibility of producing the claimed item in court, which triggers the need to specify value.
الحبس (al-habs): Imprisonment of the defendant until he produces the claimed item.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Theft
B) Loan (qard)
C) Ghasb (usurpation)
D) Sale (bai')

Key Takeaway
Value specification is only required when the item cannot be produced, except in ghasb and rahn where it is never required.

Istishab: A Proof for Defence, Not Establishment
Critique of istishab and its limited application

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position that istishab is a proof only for defence, and apply the ruling on bringing an animal into the mosque when necessary.

Core Idea
Istishab defends existing rights but does not create them; necessity can permit acts otherwise prohibited.

This lesson explores the usul al-fiqh principle of istishab (presumption of continuity) and its distinct treatment in the Hanafi school. Unlike the Shafi'i school, which accepts istishab for both establishing and defending claims, the Hanafis restrict it to defence. The passage then illustrates this principle with a practical case: bringing a disputed animal into the mosque so the judge can examine it. This example shows how necessity can override general prohibitions without creating new rights.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Legal Principle
The passage names istishab as the relevant usul principle. Understand its definition: the presumption that a previous state continues until proven otherwise.
Istishab is a debated proof across madhahib.
2
State the Hanafi Position
The text states that istishab is a proof for defence (daf'), not for establishing a claim (ithbat). This is because the original evidence (dalil) that established the state does not guarantee its continuation.
Hanafi: istishab is hujja fi al-daf' la fi al-ithbat.
3
Contrast with the Shafi'i View
The author of al-Tawdih notes that al-Shafi'i accepts istishab for both defence and establishment when doubt arises about continuity after initial proof.
This ikhtilaf is methodological.
4
Apply Istishab to the Animal Case
The passage gives a scenario: a disputed animal at the mosque door, the judge cannot see it from outside, and witnesses/claimant cannot point to it. To adjudicate, the judge orders it brought in. This is permitted due to necessity (hajah). The Prophet's tawaf on his camel in the Sacred Mosque is cited as evidence that necessity permits temporary entry.
The permissibility is conditional on need.
5
Extract the Ruling and its Basis
The ruling is: bringing an animal into the mosque is permissible when necessary for adjudication. The basis is the prophetic action and the principle that necessity permits otherwise prohibited acts. Istishab is not used to establish a right here; rather, it is a defence of the action based on necessity.
The ruling is mubah with a condition.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم اعلم أنه ينبغي لأن ما ذكره سمى في علم الأصول استصحاباً وهو حجة في الدفع لا في الإثبات، ولا شك أن ما ذكر من قبيل الإثبات قال صاحب التوضيح : ومن الحجج الفاسدة الاستصحاب وهو حجة عند الشافعي في كل ما يثبت وجوده بدليل ثم وقع الشك في بقائه، وعندنا حجة للدفع لا للإثبات إذ الدليل الموجب لا يدل على البقاء وهذا ظاهر قوله : ( وفي الدابة يخير القاضي الخ) وقال في غاية البيان، فإن كان دابة ولا يقع بصر القاضي ولا يتأتى الإشارة من الشهود والمدعي وهي على باب المسجد يأمر بإدخالها فإنه جائز عند الحاجة ألا ترى أنه عليه الصلاة والسلام طاف بالبيت على ناقته مع أن حرمة المسجد الحرام فوق حرمة سائر المساجد وإن كان يقع بصر القاضي عليها فلا يدخلها لأنه لا يأمن ما يكون منها والحاجة منعدمة اهـ .
Then know that what he mentioned is called in usul al-fiqh istishab, and it is a proof in defence, not in establishing a claim. Without doubt, what is mentioned is of the type of establishment. The author of al-Tawdih said: Among the invalid proofs is istishab, and it is a proof according to al-Shafi'i in everything whose existence is established by evidence and then doubt occurs about its continuance. According to us, it is a proof for defence, not for establishment, because the obligating evidence does not indicate continuance. This is apparent from his statement: (And regarding the animal, the judge has an option...) And he said in Ghayat al-Bayan: If it is an animal and the judge's sight does not fall upon it, and pointing by witnesses and the claimant is not possible, and it is at the mosque door, he orders it to be brought in, for it is permissible when there is need. Do you not see that the Prophet (peace be upon him) performed tawaf around the House on his camel, even though the sanctity of the Sacred Mosque is greater than that of other mosques? If the judge's sight falls upon it, he does not bring it in because he is not safe from what it might do, and the need is absent.
Source pages p. 337

Commentary
This passage discusses the usul principle of istishab (presumption of continuity). The commentator clarifies that istishab is valid only for defence (daf') not for establishing a claim (ithbat). In the context of a claim about an absent item, the claimant cannot rely on istishab to prove his right. The author of al-Tawdih criticizes istishab as an invalid proof in this context, noting that al-Shafi'i accepts it for continuity, but the Hanafis only accept it for defence. The passage then gives an example: if a disputed animal is at the mosque door and cannot be pointed to from outside, the judge may order it brought into the mosque due to necessity, based on the Prophet's act of performing tawaf on his camel inside the Sacred Mosque. However, if the judge can see the animal outside, there is no need to bring it in. This illustrates that necessity can override general prohibitions, but it does not establish a substantive right.

Key Terms
استصحاب (istishab): Presumption of continuity of a state until proven otherwise; here it is valid only for defence.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) It is always prohibited because the mosque is sacred.
B) It is permissible only when there is a need, as shown by the Prophet's tawaf on his camel.
C) It is permissible only if the animal is small and clean.
D) It is obligatory to bring it in to establish the right.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school restricts istishab to defence, and the permissibility of bringing an animal into the mosque is based on necessity, not on establishing a claim.

Boundaries Requirement in Real Estate Claims & Intra-Hanafi Ikhtilaf
Ruling that boundaries must be specified in a real estate claim due to inability to point

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state why boundaries must be specified in a Hanafi real estate claim, distinguish the positions of the author and the Sahibayn regarding well-known property, and apply the preferred method of describing property (general to specific).

Core Idea
Boundary specification is wajib in real estate claims because pointing is impossible; witness knowledge may exempt, but the claimant's duty remains.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq explains that a claimant of real estate must mention the property's boundaries, since the land cannot be physically presented in court. The same requirement applies to witnesses, but an exception appears in al-Multaqat: if the witnesses already know the property, boundary details are not needed. The passage also discusses the proper order of description—starting with the most general location (city) and moving to the specific (street)—following Imam Muhammad's method. Understanding this ruling is essential for correctly filing a property claim in a Hanafi court.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the ruling
The act is claiming real estate. The ruling is that boundaries must be specified (wajib).
The ruling applies to both claimant and witnesses, but the exception for witnesses is noted.
2
Locate the dalil
The proof is the inability to point to the property (ta'adhdhur al-ta'rif bil-isharah). This is an aqli (rational) proof based on the nature of real estate.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
Since pointing is impossible, specification (tahdid) becomes the only means of identification, making it compulsory.
4
Note the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani hold that if the property is well-known and witnesses recognize it, boundaries are not required. The author (Al-Marghinani) insists on boundaries even then.
This ikhtilaf is recorded in Munyat al-Mufti.
5
Determine the mufta bihi
The relied-upon position is that boundaries must be specified in the claim, but witness identification may suffice. The method of description should follow Muhammad's principle: start with the most general (town) then the specific (street, etc.).
Jami' al-Fusulayn prefers Imam Muhammad's method.

قوله : (وإن ادعى عقاراً ذكر حدوده لأنه تعذر التعريف بالإشارة لتعذر النقل فيصار إلى التحديد، وكما يشترط التحديد في الدعوى يشترط في الشهادة وفي الملتقط : وإذا عرف الشهود الدار بعينها جاز وإن لم يذكروا حدودها .ا.هـ. أطلقه فشمل ما إذا كان العقار مشهوراً فلا بد من تحديده عنده خلافاً لهما كذا في منية المفتي : ولم يشترط المؤلف لدعوى العقار غير التحديد وفي جامع الفصولين في دعوى العقار: لا بد أن يذكر بلدة فيها الدار ثم المحلة ثم السكة فيبدأ أولاً بذكر الكورة ثم المحلة اختياراً لقول محمد فإن مذهبه أن يبدأ أولاً بالأعم ثم بالأخص فالأخص . وقيل : يبدأ بالأخص ثم بالأعم فيقول دار في سكة كذا في محلة كذا في كورة كذا، وقاسه على النسب فيقال فلان ثم يقال ابن فلان ثم يذكر الجد فبدأ بما هو أقرب فيترقى إلى الأبعد وقول محمد أحسن إذ العام يعرف بالخاص لا بالعكس وفصل النسب حجة عليه إذ الأعم اسمه فإن أحمد في الدنيا كثير فإن عرف وإلا ترقى إلى الأخص فيقول ابن محمد فإن عرف وإلا ترقى إلى الجد ا هـ . ثم قال : يكتب في الحد ثم ينتهي إلى كذا أيضاً. وفي الدرر قال في الكافي : وإن لم يبين القيمة وقال غصبت مني عين كذا ولا أدري أهو هالك أو قائم ولا أدري كم كانت قيمته ذكر في عامة الكتب أنه تسمع دعواه لأن الإنسان ربما لا يعلم قيمة ما له فلو كلف بيان القيمة لتضرر به أقول : فائدة صحة الدعوى هذه الجهالة الفاحشة مع توجه اليمين على الخصم إذا أنكر والجبر على البيان إذا أقر أو نكل عن اليمين فليتأمل، فإن كلام الكافي لا يكون كافياً إلا بهذا التحقيق .اهـ. وقوله «فائدتها توجه اليمين» أي حيث لا بينة وإلا ففائدتها الحبس كما علمت قوله : ( وإن لم يكن له حمل يصح قال في نور العين بعد هذه العبارة : وفي غصب غير المثلي وإهلاكه ينبغي أن يبين قيمته يوم غصبه في ظاهر الرواية، وفي رواية يتخير المالك أخذ قيمته غصبه أو هلاكه فلا بد من بيان أنها قيمة أي اليومين، ولو ادعى ألف دينار
His statement: (And if he claims real estate, he mentions its boundaries because definition by pointing is impossible due to inability to transport, so one resorts to specification. Just as specification is required in the claim, it is required in testimony. In al-Multaqat: If the witnesses know the house by its identity, it is permissible even if they do not mention its boundaries. He made it absolute, so it includes when the real estate is well-known; it must still be specified according to him, contrary to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad). Thus in Munyat al-Mufti: The author did not require other than specification for a real estate claim. And in Jami' al-Fusulayn regarding a real estate claim: He must mention the town in which the house is located, then the neighborhood, then the street. He begins with the district then the neighborhood, choosing the saying of Muhammad, for his madhhab is to start with the more general then the more specific. It is said: start with the more specific then the more general, saying 'a house in such street in such neighborhood in such district,' and analogized to lineage: one says 'so-and-so' then 'son of so-and-so' then 'grandfather,' starting with the closest and ascending. The saying of Muhammad is better, for the general is known by the specific, not vice versa. The detailed lineage is evidence against it, for the general name: 'Ahmad' is many in the world; if known, fine; otherwise one ascends to the specific: 'son of Muhammad'; if known, fine; otherwise ascends to the grandfather.
Source pages p. 337

Commentary
This passage deals with the conditions for a valid claim (da'wa) of real estate. The author's statement from the matn says that when claiming real estate, one must mention its boundaries because the property cannot be physically brought to court, so description replaces pointing. The same requirement applies to witnesses. However, there is a nuance from al-Multaqat: if the witnesses know the property by its identity (e.g., they recognize the house), they do not need to state boundaries. The commentator notes that according to one view (the author's), even if the property is well-known, boundaries must be specified, unlike the position of Abu Yusuf and Muhammad. In Jami' al-Fusulayn, the method of description is given: the claimant should first mention the city/town, then the district (mahallah), then the street (sikkah). Imam Muhammad's preferred method is to start with the most general and then go to the most specific, because the general is known through the specific, not the reverse. An alternative view says start with the most specific. The commentator prefers Muhammad's view, analogizing to lineage identification where one says the individual's name first and then ascends to the father only if needed. The passage also digresses into a discussion about value specification in ghasb claims from al-Kafi, but the main ruling on real estate boundaries is clear: specification is required, but if the property is well-known and witnesses identify it without boundaries, it may suffice.

Key Terms
التحديد (al-tahdid): Specifying the boundaries of real estate in a claim.
الملتقط (al-Multaqat): A Hanafi fiqh work; its ruling that witness identification suffices without boundaries is noted here.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) To determine the exact price.
B) Because the property cannot be physically brought to court.
C) To prove ownership.
D) To avoid false claims.

Key Takeaway
The specification of boundaries is wajib in real estate claims; witness knowledge may provide an exception, but the claimant must still describe the property's location methodically.

Classification of Buildings and Palm Trees for Shuf'a
Statement by Hanafi masters that buildings and palm trees are movable, not subject to pre-emption

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on shuf'a for buildings and palm trees, and distinguish when shuf'a applies based on whether they are sold with or without land.

Core Idea
Buildings and palm trees are movables for shuf'a; no pre-emption if sold alone.

In Hanafi fiqh, the right of pre-emption (shuf'a) is designed to protect co-owners and neighbors from unwanted outsiders, but it only applies to immovable property (real estate). This lesson clarifies a key classification: buildings and palm trees are considered movable (manqul) for shuf'a purposes, even though they are physically attached to the land. Understanding this distinction is essential for correctly applying shuf'a in property transactions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the legal issue
The passage addresses whether shuf'a (pre-emption) applies when buildings or palm trees are sold. Determine the core question: are these items considered real estate (aqar) or movables (manqulat) for shuf'a?
2
State the Hanafi ruling
The Hanafi masters (mashayikhuna) explicitly state that buildings and palm trees are from the movables. Therefore, if sold separately without the land, there is no shuf'a. This is the mufta bihi (operative ruling).
The ruling relies on the classification as manqul, not aqar.
3
Identify the exception
If buildings or palm trees are sold together with the land (arsa), shuf'a applies to the land, and the buildings/trees follow as dependencies (tābi'). The right of pre-emption is established for the land, not the structures themselves.
The exception confirms the general rule: the land triggers shuf'a, not the structures.
4
Note the intra-school disagreement
The author criticizes some contemporary scholars who mistakenly considered palm trees as real estate (aqar) and gave fatwa accordingly. This indicates that although the school is clear, some later scholars held a different view. The correct position is that palm trees are manqul for shuf'a.
The disagreement may stem from a different definition of aqar for other purposes, but for shuf'a the Hanafi school is consistent.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقد صرح مشايخنا في كتاب الشفعة بأن البناء والنخل من المنقولات وأنه لا شفعة فيهما إذا بيعا بلا عرصة فإن بيعا معها وجبت تبعاً وسيأتي بيانه إن شاء الله تعالى فيها. وقد غلط بعض العصريين فجعل النخيل من العقار وأفتى به ونبه فلم يرجع كعادته.
And our masters have clearly stated in the book of Shuf'a that buildings and palm trees are from the movables, and there is no pre-emption in them if they are sold without a vacant lot. If they are sold with it, it becomes obligatory as a consequence. And its explanation will come, God willing. And some moderns erred and considered palm trees as real estate and gave fatwa accordingly, and was warned but did not retract, as is his habit.
Source pages p. 339

Commentary
This passage states a clear ruling in the Hanafi school regarding shuf'a (pre-emption). Buildings built on land and palm trees are considered movable property (manqulat) for the purposes of shuf'a. Therefore, if they are sold separately without the land (arṣa), there is no right of pre-emption. However, if they are sold together with the land, the right of pre-emption applies to the land and the buildings/trees follow as dependencies. The author criticizes some contemporary scholars who mistakenly classified palm trees as real estate (aqar) and gave fatwa on that basis, despite being corrected.

Key Terms
الشفعة (al-shuf'a): Pre-emption: the right to purchase property that is being sold to an outsider, held by a co-owner or neighbor.
العرصة (al-'arṣa): Vacant lot, the land itself.
المنقولات (al-manqūlāt): Movables; assets that can be moved without damaging their substance.
العقار (al-'aqār): Real estate; immovable property like land and buildings that are permanently affixed.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, when does shuf'a apply to palm trees?
A) When sold alone
B) When sold with the land
C) Never
D) Only if the buyer is a stranger

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, buildings and palm trees are movables for shuf'a, so no pre-emption if sold without land; if sold with land, shuf'a applies to the land and they follow as dependencies.

Sufficiency of Three Boundaries in a Land Claim
Ruling on the number of boundaries required in a claim for real estate

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on the number of boundaries required in a land claim, distinguish it from the positions of Zufar and Abu Yusuf, and explain the thamarah al-khilaf.

Core Idea
Three boundaries suffice because the fourth is known by extension – the majority opinion outweighs full specification.

In property claims, specifying boundaries is essential to define the subject matter. But how many boundaries must be given? The Hanafi school holds that three are enough, while Zufar insists on four and Abu Yusuf accepts two. This lesson explores the reasoning behind these positions and the practical consequences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the issue
The central question: how many boundaries of a land parcel must be specified for a claim to be valid?
2
State the majority Hanafi position
The text says 'three suffice' because the fourth can be inferred from the alignment of the other three – the majority is present.
This is the mufta bihi.
3
Identify the opposing views
Zufar requires all four boundaries; Abu Yusuf holds that two are sufficient as per practice.
4
Analyze the thamarah al-khilaf
Practical consequence: a claim with three boundaries is valid; one with only two is valid according to Abu Yusuf but not the majority.
5
Understand the error scenario
If the witness errs in the fourth boundary, the claim fails because the object changes; but mere omission of the fourth does not invalidate. Error must be admitted by the witness, not claimed by the defendant.
From Jami' al-Fusulayn.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: (وكفت ثلاثة لوجود الأكثر خلافاً لزفر، وعند أبي يوسف يكتفى باثنين كما في الخانية، بخلاف ما إذا غلط في الرابع لأنه يختلف المدعى به ولا كذلك بتركه. وفي جامع الفصولين وإنما يثبت الغلط بإقرار الشاهد أني غلطت فيه، أما لو ادعاه المدعى عليه لا تسمع ولا تقبل بينته لأن دعوى غلط الشاهد من المدعى عليه إنما يكون بعد دعوى المدعي وجواب المدعى عليه والمدعي عليه حين أجاب المدعي فقد صدقه أن المدعي بهذه الحدود فيصير المدعي بدعوى الغلط مناقضاً بعده أو نقول: تفسير دعوى الغلط أحد الحدود أن يقول المدعى عليه أحد الحدود ليس ما ذكره الشاهد أو يقول صاحب الحد ليس بهذا بالاسم الذي ذكره الشاهد وكل ذلك نفي والشهادة على النفي لا تقبل ا هـ .
His statement: (And three suffice, because the majority is present, contrary to Zufar, and according to Abu Yusuf two suffice as in al-Khaniyya, unlike if he errs in the fourth because the claimed matter differs, and not so if he omits it. And in Jami' al-Fusulayn: The error is established only by the witness's admission 'I erred in it'; as for the defendant claiming it, it is not heard nor is his evidence accepted, because the defendant's claim of the witness's error is only after the plaintiff's claim and the defendant's response, and when the defendant answered the plaintiff he confirmed that the plaintiff's claim is with these boundaries, so the plaintiff becomes contradictory with his subsequent claim of error. Or we say: The interpretation of the claim of error of one boundary is that the defendant says 'One boundary is not what the witness mentioned' or says 'The owner of the boundary is not the person named by the witness,' and all that is negation, and testimony for negation is not accepted. End.)
Source pages p. 339

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute on the minimal number of boundaries required in a land claim. The base rule is that three boundaries suffice because the fourth can be determined by extension (the majority opinion). Zufar disagreed, requiring all four. Abu Yusuf went further, allowing two boundaries. The text then clarifies that if the witness errs in the fourth boundary, it invalidates the claim because the claimed object changes, whereas omission of the fourth does not. The commentary from Jami' al-Fusulayn explains that the error must be established by the witness's own admission, not by the defendant's claim, because the defendant's initial response (confirming the boundaries) constitutes an affirmation, making a subsequent claim of error contradictory. Any attempt to prove error via testimony would be testimony for a negation, which is inadmissible.

Key Terms
حد (hadd): Boundary or limit of a piece of land.
دعوى (da'wa): A legal claim or lawsuit before a judge.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, how many boundaries suffice in a claim for land?
A) One
B) Two
C) Three
D) Four

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, three boundaries are sufficient for a land claim; the fourth is implied by extension of the others.

The Condition of Mentioning the Defendant's Possession
Condition for lawsuit based on possession

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the condition of mentioning the defendant's possession in a Hanafi lawsuit and distinguish the additional requirement for movable property.

Core Idea
A lawsuit is only valid if the plaintiff alleges the defendant's possession; for movables, he must also allege it is without right.

This passage from Bahr al-Ra'iq clarifies a crucial procedural condition in Hanafi litigation. The plaintiff must explicitly claim that the disputed property is in the defendant's possession to establish proper adversary. The commentator distinguishes between movable and immovable property, adding a further requirement for movables because they can be lawfully possessed by non-owners.

Guided Path
1
Identify the base text phrase
Locate the phrase 'وأنه في يده' (and that it is in his hand) in the matn of Kanz al-Daqā'iq. This is the core condition being explained.
The phrase is a direct quote from the base text.
2
Understand why possession is a condition
The defendant becomes a party to the lawsuit only because he possesses the property. If it is not in his possession, there is no lawsuit between them.
Possession creates the legal nexus for the claim.
3
Distinguish between movable and immovable
The commentator states he did not restrict the condition to immovables, contrary to another commentator. The condition applies to both categories, but for movables an extra condition exists.
The pronoun 'it' in the phrase refers to both movables and immovables.
4
Note the additional requirement for movables
For movables, the plaintiff must say 'in his hand without right' because a movable can be lawfully possessed (e.g., as a pawn). The claim must assert wrongful possession.
This ensures the lawsuit targets unlawful detention, not lawful holding.
5
Recognize the commentator's refinement
The commentator (likely Ibn Nujaym) clarifies his own position: he did not narrow the base rule to immovables, and he emphasizes the additional phrase for movables.
This shows how commentators may refine or differ from previous scholars.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (وأنه في يده) أي وذكر المدعي أن المدعى به في يد المدعى عليه لأنه إنما يصير خصماً بكونه في يده فإن لم يكن في فلا خصومة بينهما. وإنما جعلت الضمير عائداً إلى المدعي الشامل للمنقول والعقار ولم يده أخصصه بالعقار كما فعل الشارح لكونه شرطاً فيهما، وفي المنقول يجب أن يقول في يده بغير حق إذ الشيء قد يكون في يد غير المالك بحق كالرهن في يد المرتهن.
His statement: (And that it is in his hand) means the plaintiff must mention that the claimed property is in the defendant's possession, because the defendant only becomes a party to the lawsuit by virtue of being in possession. If it is not in his possession, there is no lawsuit between them. The pronoun (it) is made to refer to the plaintiff encompassing both movable and immovable property, and I did not restrict it to immovable as the commentator did, because it is a condition for both. In movables, the plaintiff must say 'in his hand without right' because a thing may be in someone's hand lawfully, like a pawn in the hand of the pledgee.
Source pages p. 340

Commentary
This passage is a ruling explanation from the commentary on Kanz al-Daqā'iq. The commentator parses the phrase 'وأنه في يده' from the base text. He explains that the plaintiff must explicitly state that the claimed item is in the defendant's possession. This is a necessary condition (shart) for the lawsuit to be heard; without it, there is no proper adversary. The pronoun 'it' refers to the claimed property, whether movable or immovable. The commentator clarifies that he did not restrict this condition to immovable property alone, contrary to what the main commentator (al-Shārīḥ) did. For movable property, an additional requirement exists: the plaintiff must say 'bi ghayr ḥaqq' (without right) because movable property can be lawfully possessed by a non-owner, such as a pawn in the hands of a pledgee. This ensures that the claim is against wrongful possession.

Key Terms
يد (yad): Possession or control of property, which is a key factor in determining the proper defendant in a lawsuit.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what must a plaintiff state in a claim for a movable item?
A) That the defendant is in possession without specifying right
B) That the defendant is in possession without right
C) That the defendant is the owner
D) That the defendant has lawful possession

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi lawsuits, the plaintiff must always allege the defendant's possession; for movables, an additional allegation of wrongful possession is required.

Conditions for Identifying Property and Persons in a Lawsuit
Defines how to identify property boundaries and persons in a lawsuit

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rules for identifying property boundaries and persons in a lawsuit, and distinguish between sufficient and insufficient descriptions.

Core Idea
A claim is invalid if the subject is ambiguously identified—specificity is required to avoid gross ignorance.

In legal proceedings, the subject matter must be clearly identified. This lesson explores Hanafi rules for describing property and persons, including boundaries and the sufficiency of identification by profession or name. Understanding these requirements ensures a valid lawsuit.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
Recognize the act of describing property or naming a party in a claim, and determine when it is valid.
The ruling is that insufficient identification invalidates the claim.
2
Extract the conditions for valid identification
From the text, note required conditions: boundaries must be specified; if a boundary is land of unknown owner, the name of the possessor must be mentioned; if land of the kingdom, it is sufficient without possessor.
For persons: names, kunyah, or profession may suffice under certain conditions.
3
Trace the intra-Hanafi dispute
Notice the ikhtilaf between Imam Abu Hanifa and the two disciples (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) regarding identification by profession alone.
Imam Abu Hanifa: not sufficient. Two disciples: sufficient if the person is well-known.
4
Apply the operative fatwa
Understand that the purpose is notification, and the mufta bihi is that three names (person, master, father) suffice, and profession suffices if well-known per the two disciples.
Shaykh al-Islam's view supports this.

قال لزيق ملك ورثة فلان لا يكفي إذ الورثة مجهولون منهم ذو فرض ومنهم ذو رحم فجهلت فاحشة ألا ترى أن الشهادة بأن هذا وارث فلان لا تقبل لجهالته في الوارث، وقيل لو كتب لزيق أرض ورثة فلان قبل القسمة قيل يصح وقيل لا . كتب لزيق دار من يصح . تركة فلان حداً؛ كذا في جامع الفصولين. ثم قال: لو جعل أحد حدوده أرضاً لا يصح يدري مالكها لا يكفي ما لم يقل هو في يد فلان حتى تحصل المعرفة، ولو جعل أحد الحدود أرض المملكة يصح ولو لم يذكر أنه في يد من لأن أرض المملكة في يد السلطان بواسطة يد نائبه المختار أنه لو ذكر اسم ذي اليد يكفي لو كان الحد أرضاً لا يدري مالكه ا هـ. المؤلف إلى أن ذكر الكنية بالأب أو الابن لا تكفي عن الجد إلا إذا كان مشهوراً كأبي حنيفة وابن أبي ليلى ا هـ . وفي البزازية من كتاب القاضي إلى القاضي أن التعريف بالحرفة لا يكفي عند الإمام، وعندهما إن كان معروفاً بالصناعة كفى، وإن نسبها إلى زوجها يكفي والمقصود الإعلام، ولو ذكر اسم المولى واسم أبيه لا غير ذكر السرخسي أنه لا يكفي، وذكر شيخ الإسلام أنه يكفي وبه يفتى لحصول التعريف بذكر ثلاثة العبد والمولى وأبوه ا بيان أسماء أصحاب الحدود أن يكون كذلك. وفي الملتقط وربما لا يحصل إلا بذكر الجد وإذا لم يعرف جده لا يميز عن غيره إلا بذكر مواليه أو ذكر حرفته أو وطنه أو دكانه أو حليته فإن التمييز هو المقصود فليحصل بما قل أو كثر وأما حكم الشهادة بالمحدود ففي دعوى الخانية عن شمس الأئمة الحلواني أنه على ثلاثة أوجه في فصل دعوى الدور والأراضي فليراجع من أراده في شهادة الخزانة رجل أشهد على ملك دار بعينها إلا أنه لا يعرف حدودها يجوز له أن يسأل الثقات عن حدودها للشهادة ولكن يشهد بالدار على إقراره ولا يشهد بذكر الحدود على إقراره حتى لا يكون كاذباً اهـ.
Book of Claims. And he indicated, and its analogy in: He said: 'To the heir of the estate of so-and-so' is not sufficient because the heirs are unknown, among them one who is an obligatory share holder and among them one who is a uterine relative, so the ignorance is gross. Do you not see that testimony that this one is an heir of so-and-so is not accepted due to ignorance about the heir? And it was said: If he wrote 'to the heir of the land of so-and-so' before division, it is said it is correct, and it is said it is not. He wrote 'to the heir of the house of so-and-so' is correct. 'The estate of so-and-so' as a boundary; thus in Jami' al-Fusulayn. Then he said: If he made one of its boundaries a land, it is not correct if he does not know its owner; it is not sufficient unless he says it is in the hand of so-and-so until knowledge is attained. And if he made one of the boundaries the land of the kingdom, it is correct even if he did not mention in whose hand it is, because the land of the kingdom is in the hand of the sultan through the hand of his deputy. The chosen opinion is that if he mentions the name of the possessor it is sufficient, if the boundary was a land whose owner is unknown. End quote. The author: And mentioning the kunyah by father or son does not suffice instead of the grandfather unless he is famously known, like Abu Hanifa and Ibn Abi Layla. End quote. In al-Bazzaziyyah, from the chapter on writing of the judge to the judge, that identification by profession is not sufficient according to Imam (Abu Hanifa). According to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), if he is well known by his craft it is sufficient. And if she attributes it to her husband, it is sufficient, and the purpose is notification. And if he mentioned the name of the master and his father only, al-Sarakhsi said it is not sufficient, and Shaykh al-Islam said it is sufficient, and according to him fatwa is given because identification is achieved by mentioning three: the slave, the master, and his father. Explanation of the names of the owners of boundaries should be like that. And in al-Multaqat, and perhaps it is not achieved except by mentioning the grandfather, and if his grandfather is not known he is not distinguished from others except by mentioning his masters or his profession or his homeland or his shop or his description, because distinction is the purpose, so it is achieved by little or much. As for the ruling of testimony regarding the bounded property, in Da'wa al-Khaniyyah from Shams al-A'immah al-Halwani that it is on three aspects in the chapter of claims concerning houses and lands, so let those who wish refer to it. In the testimony of al-Khazanah: A man took witness upon the ownership of a specific house, except that he does not know its boundaries. It is permissible for him to ask trustworthy people about its boundaries for testimony, but he testifies to the house based on his acknowledgment and does not testify to mentioning the boundaries based on his acknowledgment so that he does not become a liar. End quote.
Source pages p. 341

Commentary
This passage from Kitab al-Da'wa (Book of Claims) in al-Bahr al-Ra'iq discusses the requirements for properly identifying the subject matter of a lawsuit, specifically when describing property boundaries or heirs. The author explains that simply stating 'to the heir of so-and-so's estate' is insufficient because the identity of the heir is unknown—there could be both Quranic share holders and uterine relatives. The same applies to testimony about inheritance: one cannot say 'this is the heir of so-and-so' without knowing which heir. When a property boundary is described as 'land belonging to unknown owner', it is invalid unless the name of the person in whose hand it is mentioned, because that provides identification. However, if the boundary is 'royal land' (ard al-mamlakah), it is valid even without mentioning a possessor because it is known to be in the sultan's hand. The chosen opinion is that mentioning the name of the possessor suffices. The discussion extends to identifying persons: a kunyah (e.g., Abu Hanifa) suffices without the grandfather if the person is famously known. Among Hanafi imams, there is an ikhtilaf: Imam Abu Hanifa holds that identifying by profession alone is insufficient, while Abu Yusuf and Muhammad say it suffices if the person is well-known by that profession. Similarly, identifying by the father's name alone may not suffice; the preferred fatwa is that three names (the person, his master, and the father) are sufficient. The purpose is notification, so any means that achieves distinction is acceptable. Finally, the passage notes that a witness who does not know the boundaries may ask trustworthy people, but he should testify only based on the owner's acknowledgment, not on his own knowledge of boundaries, to avoid false testimony.

Key Terms
ذو فرض (dhu fard): An heir entitled to a fixed Quranic share (e.g., spouse, parent).
التعريف بالحرفة (al-ta'reef bi al-hirafah): Identifying a person by his profession or trade.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is saying 'to the heir of so-and-so's estate' insufficient in a claim?
A) Because the heirs are unknown and include both fixed-share and uterine relatives.
B) Because the estate has not been divided yet.
C) Because the testator may have made a bequest.
D) Because the phrase is too vague and does not specify the property.

Key Takeaway
For a valid claim in Hanafi law, the property and parties must be identified with sufficient specificity to eliminate gross ignorance.

Requirement of Possession (Yad) in Property Claims
Explains necessity of proving defendant's possession for property lawsuits

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi school requires proof of possession (yad) as a condition for a property claim, and distinguish the rules for movables and immovables.

Core Idea
A property claim requires the defendant's hand; immovables need proof of hand to prevent collusion, movables rely on visible possession.

Possession (yad) is the cornerstone of property litigation. Without it, the defendant is not a proper contestant. The Hanafi school draws a critical distinction between movables and immovables, driven by the risk of collusion (tawadu'). This lesson unpacks the ruling, its proofs, and the practical consequences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the condition
The plaintiff must explicitly state that the claimed property is in the defendant's hand. This is the foundational requirement for a valid property claim.
Without this, there is no contest (khumsumah).
2
Distinguish movables and immovables
For movables, an additional phrase 'without right' (bighayr haqq) is required because the item could be lawfully in another's hand (e.g., pledge). For immovables, this extra phrase is not needed in the claim; instead, the focus is on proving the hand itself.
The 'without right' condition addresses lawful possession scenarios.
3
Understand the suspicion of collusion
Immovable property is prone to collusion because the true owner may be absent. Two individuals could collude: one admits possession, the other proves ownership, then the real owner is evicted. Thus, hand in immovables cannot be established by mutual admission; it requires evidence (bayyinah) or the judge's knowledge.
This suspicion is repelled in movables because the owner's hand is normally present.
4
Analyze the testimony rules
Testimony to ownership alone suffices for movables (for the purpose of establishing ownership) but not for demanding delivery unless the witnesses also state that it is in the defendant's hand without right. For immovables, hand must be proven by evidence or the judge's knowledge.
This nuanced rule reconciles the need to protect ownership while preventing collusion.

Source Text

في
قوله : (ولا تثبت اليد في العقار بتصادقهما بل ببينة أو علم القاضي بخلاف المنقول) نفياً لتهمة المواضعة إذ العقار عساه في يد غيرهما بخلاف المنقول لأن اليد فيه مشاهدة. قيد بالدعوى لما في شهادات البزازية : شهدوا أنه ملكه ولم يقولوا في يده بغير حق يفتى بالقبول. قال الصدر الأجل الحلواني : اختلف فيه المشايخ والصحيح أنه لا يقبل لأنه إن لم يثبت يده بغير حق لا يمكنه المطالبة بالتسليم وبه كان يفتي أكثر المشايخ. وقيل: يقضى في المنقول ولا يقضى في العقار حتى يقولوا إنه في يده بغير حق والصحيح الذي عليه الفتوى أنه يقبل في حق القضاء بالملك لا في حق المطالبة بالتسليم حتى قالوا لو سأل القاضي الشاهد أهو في يد المدعى عليه بغير حق فقال لا أدري يقبل على الملك ؛ نص عليه في المحيط. وفي دعوى البزازية معزياً إلى الصغرى ادعى على أخر ضيعة أنها له فأقر المدعى عليه أنها في يده وبرهن المدعي على أنها ملكه فحكم الحاكم بالملك له لا يصح ما لم يثبت اليد بالبينة أو يعلم الحاكم، وفيه قال المدعى عليه ليس العقار في يدي يحلفه حتى يقر فإذا أقر باليد يحلفه أنها ليست ملكه حتى يقر بالملك للمدعي، فإذا أقر له به يأمره بترك التعرض لكن إن أراد أن يبرهن أنها ملكه لا بد من تقديم البينة على أنها في يده لأن المالك قد يبعد عن العقار عادة فأمكن أن يتواضع اثنان ويقر أحدهما باليد ويبرهن الآخر عليه بالملك ويسامح في الشهود ثم يدفع المالك معللاً بحكم الحاكم وهذه التهمة في المنقول منتف لأن يد المالك لا تنقطع عن المنقول عادة بل يكون في يده فاندفع به ما قيل في شرح الوقاية تهمة المواضعة ثابتة في الموضعين على السواء فيقضى في المنقول بإقراره باليد كما صرح به جميع الكتب ا هـ. وهكذا في الخانية. وبه علم أن ثبوت اليد بالبينة أو العلم في العقار إنما هو لصحة القضاء بالملك المدعى عليه في دعوى الملك دون دعوى الضمان، وكذا دون دعوى الشراء كما سينبه عليه قوله : (فاندفع به ما قيل في شرح الوقاية أجاب في الدرر عن اعتراض الوقاية واعترضه محشوه، وللمحقق سعدى جلبي في حواشي الهداية تحقيق نفيس في هذا المحل فراجعه .
His statement: (and that it is in his hand) i.e., the plaintiff mentions that the claimed property is in the hand of the defendant because he only becomes a contestant by virtue of it being in his hand. If it is not, there is no contest between them. The pronoun is made to refer to the plaintiff, inclusive of movable and immovable property, and he did not restrict it to immovable as the commentator did because it is a condition in both. For movable property, he must say 'in his hand without right' because an item may be in the hand of a non-owner with right, such as a pledge in the hand of the pledgee. In Jami' al-Fusulayn: (If) someone usurped a slave, then another proved it is his and the judge ruled it for him, then the victim of the usurpation proved against the usurper that it is his, it is not accepted because a claim of ownership is only valid against the one in possession. But if he claims against a non-possessor saying 'you owe me', his claim is heard in respect of liability. Do you not see that his claim for liability against the first usurper is valid even if the thing itself is in the hand of the second usurper? And in the claim of a usurper of half a house undividedly: is identification by mentioning the waqf (endowment) enough if he does not mention in whose hand it is? (He counted:) If the boundary is waqf land, the repayer must be mentioned. His statement: (But if he claims against a non-possessor ...) indicates that the condition that the plaintiff state that the claimed is in the hand of the defendant is different for movable property, and that he demands it even if it is a debt he describes it and demands it. So if it is valid, is it required to clarify that the entire house is in the hand of the defendant? It is said it is required because usurpation of half undividedly only occurs if the whole is in his hand. And it is said usurpation of half undividedly is conceivable if the house is between them, so usurpation from one is usurpation of his half undividedly. End quote. He restricts to 'claim' because if they testify to a movable that it is the plaintiff's property, it is accepted even if they do not testify that it is in the defendant's hand without right, because when they testify to ownership, a person's property is not in another's hand except by some impediment, and the burden of proof is on the claimant of the impediment, not on the owner of the original. Some said: As long as they do not testify that it is in the defendant's hand without right, the defendant's hand is not cut off. The first opinion is more correct. For non-immovable property, it is not required that they testify that it is in the defendant's hand because the judge sees it in his hand, so no need for explanation; thus in al-Muhit and al-Khaniyyah.

His statement: (and the hand in immovable property is not established by their mutual agreement but by evidence or the judge's knowledge, unlike movable property) to negate the suspicion of collusion, because immovable property might be in the hand of someone else besides them, unlike movable because the hand is visible therein. He restricts to 'claim' because in Shahadat al-Bazzaziyyah: they testify that it is his property and did not say 'in his hand without right', fatwa is given for acceptance. Al-Sadr al-Ajal al-Halwani said: The scholars differed on it, and the correct view is that it is not accepted because if his hand without right is not proven, he cannot demand delivery. Most scholars used to give fatwa accordingly. And it was said: Judgment is given for movable but not for immovable until they say it is in his hand without right. The correct view upon which fatwa rests is that it is accepted for the purpose of ruling on ownership, not for demanding delivery. Thus they said: If the judge asks the witness: 'Is it in the defendant's hand without right?' and he says 'I do not know', it is accepted for ownership; it is stated in al-Muhit. And in Da'wa al-Bazzaziyyah, citing al-Sughra: Someone claimed a village from another that it was his, so the defendant admitted it was in his hand, and the plaintiff proved it was his property, so the judge ruled it as his property. This is not valid unless the hand is established by evidence or the judge knows it. And therein: If the defendant says 'the immovable is not in my hand', he is made to take an oath until he admits; when he admits the hand, he is made to take an oath that it is not his property until he admits the property to the plaintiff; when he admits it to him, he orders him to leave off harassment. But if he wants to prove it is his property, he must present evidence that it is in his hand, because the owner is usually distant from immovable property, so it is possible that two collude: one admits the hand and the other proves ownership, and they are lenient with witnesses, then the owner is evicted based on the judge's decree. This suspicion is absent in movables because the owner's hand does not normally leave movables; rather it is in his hand. By this, what was said in Sharh al-Wiqayah that the suspicion of collusion is equally present in both cases is repelled. Judgment is given in movable based on his admission of hand, as all books have clearly stated. End quote. And so in al-Khaniyyah. And by this it is known that the establishment of hand by evidence or knowledge in immovable is only for the validity of judging ownership claimed, not for liability, nor for purchase, as will be pointed out by his statement: (By this what was said in Sharh al-Wiqayah is repelled; al-Durar answered the objection of al-Wiqayah, and its annotators objected. And al-Muhaqqiq Sa'di Jilibi in his glosses on al-Hidayah has a valuable investigation on this point, so consult it.)
Source pages p. 341

Commentary
This lengthy passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq deals with the crucial requirement in a lawsuit over property: the defendant must be in possession (yad) of the claimed item. The author explains the phrase 'and that it is in his hand' from the text of Kanz al-Daqaiq. The plaintiff must state that the property is in the defendant's hand; otherwise, the defendant is not a proper contestant. For movable property, the plaintiff must add 'without right' because an item may be in someone's hand lawfully (e.g., a pledge with the pledgee). The passage then discusses the rule that a claim of ownership is only valid against the person in possession. However, a claim for damages (daman) can be made against someone who is not in possession, such as a usurper who no longer has the item. In cases of undivided share (shai' shai'an), there is a dispute whether usurpation of half requires the whole to be in the usurper's hand. The text then turns to the testimony of witnesses. A key distinction is made between movable and immovable property regarding the need for witnesses to explicitly state that the property is in the defendant's hand without right. For movables, the general rule is that testimony of ownership alone suffices because the owner's hand is normally present; the burden is on the claimant of a contrary impediment. However, there is an ikhtilaf among Hanafi scholars: some require explicit mention of possession without right for the defendant's hand to be cut, while the more correct view (as per al-Muhit) is that testimony to ownership is accepted for the purpose of establishing ownership but not for demanding delivery. For immovable property, the issue of collusion (tawadu') is more serious because the true owner may be absent. Therefore, hand in immovable is not established by the parties' mere agreement (tasaduq), but must be proven by evidence (bayyinah) or the judge's personal knowledge. The passage refutes the claim made in Sharh al-Wiqayah that the suspicion of collusion applies equally to movables and immovables, arguing that movables are always in the owner's hand physically, so that suspicion does not arise. Finally, the author directs the reader to consult other works for more detailed investigation, including al-Durar and Sa'di Jilibi's glosses on al-Hidayah.

Key Terms
اليد (al-yad): Possession or control over property; a key condition for being a defendant in a property claim.
المواضعة (al-muwada'ah): Collusion between two parties to defraud a true owner, e.g., by falsely admitting possession and proving ownership.
تصادق (tasaduq): Mutual agreement or admission between plaintiff and defendant.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) Because immovables cannot be physically moved.
B) To negate the suspicion of collusion between plaintiff and defendant.
C) Because movables are more valuable.
D) Because the judge must personally inspect the property.

Key Takeaway
In the Hanafi school, a property claim requires proof of the defendant's possession; immovables need external proof of hand to prevent collusion, while movables rely on visible possession.

Rulings on Not Returning the Oath to the Claimant
Matn statement with two specific applications regarding oaths

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi principle that the oath is not administered to the claimant and apply it to the two specific cases of deferred debt and claims against a restricted slave.

Core Idea
The oath is always on the denier, never on the claimant.

This lesson presents two concrete applications of a foundational Hanafi principle: the oath is not returned to the claimant. These rulings clarify when the defendant is or is not required to swear, based on the nature of the claim and the status of the defendant. Understanding these nuances is essential for correctly adjudicating oaths in court.

Guided Path
1
Identify the General Principle
The matn first states 'wa la turaddu yaminun 'ala mudda'i' – the oath is not returned to the claimant. Recognize this as the foundational rule.
2
Apply to Deferred Debt
Read the first application: a claim for deferred debt, defendant denies – he is NOT sworn in the stronger opinion. Why? Because the debt is not yet due, so denial is not immediate.
This ruling has an intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf; the stronger opinion is followed.
3
Apply to Restricted Slave
Read the second: a claim against a restricted slave for a right that becomes due after freedom – if the slave denies, he IS sworn. Why? Because the denial is immediate and the slave is capable of taking an oath.
The slave's interdiction does not affect his capacity to swear.
4
Note the Intra-Hanafi Ikhtilaf
The first application mentions 'azhar al-qawlayn', indicating a differing opinion within the school. The stronger view is that the denier is not sworn for a deferred debt.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بطلبه ولا ترد يمين على مدع ولا بينة لذي اليد في الملك المطلق وبينة الخارج أحق بنعم. ادعى على آخر ديناً مؤجلاً فأنكر لا يحلف في أظهر القولين. ادعى على عبد محجور حقاً يؤاخذ به بعد العتق فإن أنكر يحلف .اهـ.
With his request, and the oath is not returned to the claimant, and there is no evidence for the possessor in absolute ownership, and the outsider's evidence is more deserving. [. . .] If someone claims a deferred debt against another and he denies, he is not sworn in the stronger of the two opinions. If someone claims a right against a restricted slave that will be pursued after emancipation and he denies, then he is sworn.
Source pages p. 348

Commentary
This passage presents two rulings derived from the general principle that the oath is not returned to the claimant. The first ruling states that when a deferred debt is claimed and the defendant denies, the defendant is not required to take an oath in the stronger opinion. This indicates an intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf: the stronger view is that no oath is administered in this case. The second ruling concerns a claim against a restricted slave (mahjur) for a right that will be enforceable after emancipation. If the slave denies, he is sworn. The reason is that the slave's denial is immediate and the oath is required because he is capable of taking an oath. These rulings illustrate the application of the principle that the oath is upon the denier, not the claimant.

Key Terms
ولا ترد يمين على مدع (wa la turaddu yaminun 'ala mudda'i): The principle that the oath is never administered to the claimant; only the denier is sworn.
محجور (mahjur): A person legally restricted from certain actions, here specifically a slave under interdiction.
أظهر القولين (azhar al-qawlayn): The stronger or more preferred opinion among two within the Hanafi school.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the case of a claim for a deferred debt where the defendant denies, what is the ruling in the stronger opinion?
A) The defendant must swear
B) The claimant must swear
C) The defendant is not sworn
D) Both must swear

Key Takeaway
The oath is on the denier, never on the claimant; for a deferred debt, the stronger view is that even the denier is not sworn.

Defendant's Procedure Before Oath for Deferred Debt
Practical guidance for a defendant when asked to swear regarding a deferred debt

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the proper procedure for a defendant when asked to swear regarding a deferred debt and explain why clarifying the nature of the claim is necessary.

Core Idea
Before swearing, a defendant must ascertain if the debt is prompt or deferred to tailor the oath truthfully.

In this passage from Khizanat al-Muftin, we learn a practical ruling for a defendant facing an oath regarding a debt. The text advises the defendant to first clarify whether the claim is for a prompt or deferred debt. This nuance protects the defendant from potential perjury and aligns the oath with the actual liability.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Scenario
Consider a defendant who owes a deferred debt (not yet due) and is asked to swear before a judge regarding that debt.
The debt is nasi'ah (deferred).
2
Defendant's First Action
The defendant should ask the judge whether the claimant is claiming a prompt (hala) or deferred (nasi'ah) debt.
This clarifies the nature of the claim.
3
Consequence of Clarification
If the judge says the claim is prompt, the defendant can swear that he does not owe those specific dirhams (since the debt is not due now). This oath is truthful and suffices.
The oath is narrow to avoid lying.
4
Rationale
The defendant avoids swearing falsely about a debt that is not yet due. The ruling allows a precise oath that matches the legal reality.
It is a form of legal trick (hila) to avoid perjury.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي خزانة المفتين : من عليه دين مؤجل وأراد أن يحلفه عند القاضي ينبغي للمدعى عليه أن يسأل القاضي أن المدعي يدعي نسيئة أم حالة، فإن قال حالة يحلف بالله ما له على هذه الدراهم التي يدعيها ويسعه ذلك اهـ.
And in Khizanat al-Muftin: One who has a deferred debt and wants to swear before the judge, it is appropriate for the defendant to ask the judge whether the claimant claims a deferred or prompt debt. If he says prompt, he swears by Allah that he does not have a right to these dirhams that he claims, and that suffices him.
Source pages p. 348

Commentary
This is a practical ruling from a later fatwa collection. It addresses a scenario where a debtor is asked to swear regarding a deferred debt. The defendant is advised to first clarify with the judge whether the claim is for a prompt or deferred debt. If the claimant asserts it is prompt, the defendant can swear that he does not owe that specific amount, and this oath is sufficient. The jurist's reasoning allows for a narrower oath to avoid perjury.

Key Terms
خزانة المفتين (Khizanat al-Muftin): A well-known Hanafi fatwa collection.
نسيئة (nasi'ah): A deferred debt, where payment is postponed to a future date.
حالة (hala): A prompt or immediate debt, due at the moment of claim.
