Procedure of Oaths in Dispute Over Stolen Garment Value
Ruling on conflicting claims about value of stolen property and oaths

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling when the owner and thief disagree on the value of stolen property, and identify the oath sequence and the thief's option upon the garment's return.

Core Idea
When value is disputed, the thief's word is taken with oath, but the owner also swears to claim the higher value — a rare balance of rights.

This lesson examines a unique ruling from Imam Muhammad's Al-Istihlaf, preserved in Al-Muhit. The case presents a conflict: the victim claims the stolen garment was worth 100, while the thief admits to theft but denies that value. How does the law resolve this? The answer involves a two-step oath procedure and a post-rediscovery option for the thief. This ruling is from the Nawadir (rare transmissions) and demonstrates the Hanafi emphasis on procedural justice.

Guided Path
1
Identify the parties and their claims
The 'maghsub minhu' (victim) claims the value was 100. The 'ghasib' (thief) says he does not know the exact value but knows it is not 100.
The thief admits to the act of usurpation but disputes the quantum.
2
Determine the initial default rule
Normally, the defendant's statement is accepted with oath. Here, the thief is compelled to clarify because he admitted to an unknown value.
The thief's admission 'I know it was not 100' is a vague admission; the law requires specification.
3
Trace the oath sequence
If the thief does not clarify, he swears that he does not owe the excess claimed. Then the victim also swears that the value is 100, and takes that amount.
Both parties swear: first the defendant (thief), then the plaintiff (victim).
4
Analyze the post-recovery option
If the garment later appears, the thief may either accept the garment and pay the value, or return the garment and take back the money.
This option protects the thief from overpayment if the goods are recovered.
5
Classify the authority level
This ruling is from Imam Muhammad's Al-Istihlaf, part of the Nawadir (rare transmissions). It is a unique and subtle issue that must be memorized.
Not from Zahir al-Riwayah, but still valid and instructive.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي المحيط : ذكر محمد في الاستحلاف لو قال المغصوب منه كانت قيمة ثوبي مائة وقال الغاصب ما أدري ما قيمته ولكن علمت أن قيمته لم تكن مائة فالقول قول الغاصب مع يمينه ويجبر على البيان لأنه أقر بقيمة مجهولة، فإذا لم يبين يحلف على ما يدعي المغصوب منه في الزيادة، فإن حلف يحلف المغصوب منه أيضاً أن قيمة ثوبه مائة ويأخذ الغاصب مائة، فإذا أخذ من ثم ظهر الثوب فالغاصب بالخيار إن شاء رضي بالثوب وسلم القيمة للمغصوب منه، وإن شاء رد الثوب وأخذ القيمة وهذه من خواص هذا الكتاب وغرائب مسائله فيجب حفظها اهـ.
And in Al-Muhit: Muhammad mentioned in Al-Istihlaf: If the victim of theft said, 'The value of my garment was one hundred,' and the thief said, 'I do not know its value, but I know its value was not one hundred,' then the statement is of the thief with his oath, and he is compelled to clarify because he admitted to an unknown value. If he does not clarify, he swears regarding what the victim claims in excess. If he swears, then the victim also swears that the value of his garment is one hundred, and he takes one hundred from the thief. Then, if the garment appears after that, the thief has the choice: if he wishes, he accepts the garment and hands over the value to the victim; if he wishes, he returns the garment and takes the value. This is among the specialities of this book and rare issues, so it must be memorized.
Source pages p. 348

Commentary
This is a unique ruling from the Al-Istihlaf of Imam Muhammad (a work on oaths). The case involves conflicting claims about the value of a stolen garment. The thief admits to taking the garment but claims the value is less than what the victim states. The ruling is that the thief's statement is accepted with his oath because he is the defendant, and he is compelled to clarify the value since he admitted to some value. If he fails to clarify, he swears that he does not owe the excess claimed. If he swears, the victim also swears to the higher value and takes that amount. Later, if the garment is found intact, the thief has the option of returning the garment for a refund or keeping the value. This is a rare and subtle ruling highlighting the balance between the rights of the parties.

Key Terms
الاستحلاف (Al-Istihlaf): A work by Imam Muhammad al-Shaybani on the topic of administering oaths.
مغصوب منه (maghsunb minhu): The victim from whom property was taken (the owner).
غاصب (ghasib): The person who took the property wrongfully (the thief or usurper).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school provides a unique two-step oath procedure to resolve disputes over the value of stolen property, balancing the defendant's right to speak with the claimant's right to compensation, and granting the thief an option upon recovery.

The Oath Belongs to the Denier: Evidence from Hadith
Hadith proof and discussion about swearing witnesses

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rule that the oath is not returned to the claimant, identify the hadith evidence, and explain why witnesses are not sworn.

Core Idea
The oath belongs exclusively to the denier; the claimant and witnesses are never sworn.

This passage presents the hadith proof for the fundamental rule that the oath is not returned to the claimant. It also addresses the question of swearing witnesses, explaining that in Hanafi law the witness's own testimony (saying 'I testify') constitutes an oath, and swearing them again would disrupt rights.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling
The core ruling is: the oath is not returned to the claimant, and witnesses are not sworn. Identify this from the opening phrase 'ولا ترد يمين على مدع'.
The ruling is stated directly, then supported by hadith.
2
Locate the dalil
The Prophet ﷺ said: 'البينة على المدعي واليمين على من أنكر' (The burden of proof is on the claimant, and the oath is on the one who denies). Find this hadith in the passage.
This is a well-known hadith, accepted by all schools.
3
Analyze the wajh al-dalalah
The commentator explains that the hadith divides roles ('قسم') and the division negates partnership ('تنافي الشركة'). Oaths are exclusively for deniers, so they cannot be shared with claimants or witnesses.
The exclusive nature of the division is key.
4
Apply to witnesses
The passage then addresses the situation where a party wants the witness sworn. The Hanafi view is that the witness's own statement 'Ashhadu' (I testify) is already an oath, so swearing again would be a repetition. Also, swearing witnesses would cause them to withhold testimony and disrupt rights.
This is based on the principle that a litigant is not sworn twice.
5
Note the exception
The passage mentions the exception of li'an (mutual curse) where severity is appropriate because it resembles a hadd punishment.
This exception confirms the general rule.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بلفظه . قوله : (ولا ترد يمين على مدع لقوله عليه السلام البينة على المدعي واليمين على من أنكر» قسم والقسمة تنافي الشركة وجعل جنس الأيمان على المنكرين وليس وراء الجنس شيء. وفي البزازية: برهن على دعواه فطلب من القاضي أن يحلف المدعي أنه محق في الدعوى أو على أن الشهود صادقون أو محقون في الشهادة لا يجيبه .. قال علامة خوارزم : الخصم لا يحلف مرتين فكيف الشاهد فإن قول الشاهد أشهد يمين لأن لفظ «أشهد» عندنا وإن لم يقل بالله يمين فإذا طلب منه الشهادة في مجلس القضاء فقال أشهد فقد حلف ولا يكرر اليمين لأنا أمرنا بإكرام ..الشهود وفي التحليف تعطيل الحقوق وأن الشاهد إذا علم أن القاضي يحلفه بالمنسوخ له الامتناع عن أداء الشهادة لأنه لا يلزم عليه، ومن أقدم على الشهادة الباطلة يقدم على الحلف أيضاً غالباً لترويج الباطل، وإذا لم يحلف ورد شهادته فقد ظلم بخلاف اليمين في باب اللعان لأن كلمات اللعان جارية مجرى الحد فناسب التغليظ اهـ.
With its wording. He said: 'And the oath is not returned to the claimant' because of his saying, peace be upon him: 'The burden of proof is on the claimant, and the oath is on the one who denies.' Division and division negate partnership, and He made the genus of oaths upon the deniers, and there is nothing beyond the genus. And in Al-Bazzaziyya: If he presented evidence for his claim and then asked the judge to swear the claimant that he is right in the claim or that the witnesses are truthful or justified in their testimony, he does not respond to him... 'Allamah Khwarazm said: The litigant is not sworn twice, so how about the witness? For the witness's saying 'I testify' is an oath, because the word 'I testify' with us, even if he does not say 'by Allah,' is an oath. So if he is asked for testimony in the court and says 'I testify,' he has sworn, and the oath is not repeated, because we are commanded to respect the witnesses. And in administering the oath there is a disruption of rights, and that if the witness knows that the judge will swear him by an abrogated [form], he may refrain from giving testimony because it is not binding on him. And whoever proceeds with false testimony also proceeds with the oath often to promote falsehood. And if he is not sworn and his testimony is rejected, that is unjust, unlike the oath in the chapter of li'an, because the words of li'an are in the nature of a hadd punishment, so severity is appropriate.
Source pages p. 348

Commentary
This passage provides the hadith proof for the rule that the oath is not returned to the claimant: the Prophet said, 'The burden of proof is on the claimant, and the oath is on the one who denies.' The commentator explains that this division precludes sharing; oaths are exclusively for those who deny. It then addresses a request to swear the claimant or the witnesses. According to Al-Bazzaziyya, such a request is denied. 'Allamah Khwarazm argues that a litigant is not sworn twice, so a witness cannot be sworn either, because the witness's statement 'I testify' itself constitutes an oath in Hanafi law even without saying 'by Allah.' Swearing witnesses would harm the process by causing witnesses to withhold testimony, and those who give false testimony would likely also swear falsely. Not swearing them avoids injustice. The exception is li'an (mutual curse) where severity is appropriate because it resembles a hadd punishment.

Key Terms
قسم (qasm): Division; the hadith divides the roles between claimant and denier.
أشهد (ashhadu): I testify; in Hanafi law, this word is considered an oath even without 'by Allah.'

Key Terms from Source Passage

D) Both claimant and denier

Key Takeaway
The oath is exclusively for the denier, and even witnesses are not sworn because their testimony itself constitutes an oath.

Consequences of Nukul and Presenting the Oath Thrice
Rulings on nukul and oath presentation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling when a defendant refuses to swear (nukul) and distinguish between the recommended and obligatory aspects of presenting the oath.

Core Idea
A single refusal to swear leads to a ruling against the denier; the thrice presentation is recommended, not obligatory.

In judicial proceedings, the oath is a decisive tool. When the defendant refuses to swear (nukul) or remains silent, the Hanafi school holds that the judge immediately rules against him. Presenting the oath three times is a recommended practice, not a requirement. This lesson also covers related sub-rulings about invalid conditional oaths and when the claimant can be asked to swear.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act of nukul
Nukul is the defendant's refusal to take the oath after being asked. The text says 'if he refused once without swearing or remained silent'.
The refusal can be explicit or through silence.
2
Apply the consequence
The judge decides against the defendant (qada lahu). No second or third offer is required.
The ruling is immediate after one refusal.
3
Understand the recommendation of thrice presentation
Presenting the oath three times is sunnah (nadban), not wajib. The judge may do so out of caution, but it is not binding.
This clarifies a common misunderstanding: thrice is recommended, not mandatory.
4
Analyze the invalid conditional oath
If a debtor says 'If you swear, I will pay', the condition is void because the oath belongs to the denier, not the claimant. Payment under such condition is invalid, and the payer can reclaim.
Imam Muhammad's ruling illustrates the principle 'al-yamin ala man ankara' (the oath is on the denier).
5
Recognize when the claimant is not sworn
From Al-Qunyah: the possessor (defendant in a property dispute) cannot request that the claimant be sworn that he does not know about the building. The oath is not shifted to the claimant.
This reinforces that the oath remains on the denier only.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقضى له إن نكل مرة بلا أحلف أو سكت وعرض اليمين ثلاثاً ندباً ولا يستحلف في الواقعات الحسامية قبيل الرهن وعن محمد : من قال لآخر لي عليك ألف درهم فقال له الآخر إن حلفت أنها لك علي أديتها إليك فحلف فأداها إليه المدعى عليه إن كان أداها إليه على الشرط الذي شرطا فهو باطل، وللمؤدي أن يرجع فيما أدى لأن ذلك الشرط باطل لأنه على خلاف حكم الشرع لأن حكم الشرع أن اليمين على من أنكر دون المدعي اهـ. وفي القنية : لو أن ذا اليد طلب من القاضي استحلاف المدعي ما تعلم أني بنيت بناء هذه الدار لا يجيبه اهـ.
And he judged against him if he refused once without swearing or remained silent, and presenting the oath three times is recommended. And he is not sworn in Al-Waqi'at al-Husamiyya near the chapter of pledge. And from Muhammad: He who said to another, 'I have one thousand dirhams due from you,' and the other said, 'If you swear that it is due from me, I will pay it to you,' so he swore and the defendant paid it to him. If he paid it to him on the condition they stipulated, it is void. And the payer may reclaim what he paid because that condition is void, being contrary to the ruling of Shari'ah. For the ruling of Shari'ah is that the oath is on the denier, not the claimant. And in Al-Qunyah: If the possessor asked the judge to swear the claimant that he does not know that I built the construction of this house, he does not respond to him.
Source pages p. 349

Commentary
This passage covers several rulings. First, if the defendant refuses to swear (nukul) once or remains silent, the judge decides against him. Presenting the oath three times is recommended (nadban), not obligatory. Second, a ruling from Imam Muhammad: if a debtor conditions payment on the creditor's oath, and the creditor swears, the payment is void because the condition is invalid - the rightful oath is on the denier, not the claimant. The payer can reclaim what he paid. Third, from Al-Qunyah: if the possessor of property asks the judge to swear the claimant that he does not know the possessor built the house, the judge does not respond because the oath is not on the claimant.

Key Terms
نكل (nakala): To refuse to take an oath after being asked; nukul is the act of refusal.
ندباً (nadban): As a recommended act; presenting the oath three times is sunnah, not wajib.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway

Preference of Outsider's Evidence Over Possessor's in Absolute Ownership
Inter-madhhab dispute on evidence preference in absolute ownership

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi school prefers an outsider's evidence over a possessor's evidence in absolute ownership and evaluate the Shafi'i counter-argument.

Core Idea
In absolute ownership, the possessor's evidence is redundant – the outsider's evidence adds new proof.

When two people claim ownership of the same property, and one is in possession, whose evidence should the judge accept? The Hanafi school answers that the outsider's evidence is preferred, while Imam al-Shafi'i gives greater weight to the possessor's evidence because it is supported by the fact of possession. This lesson dissects the reasoning behind each position and the important exception for earlier dates.

Guided Path
1
Identify the contested ruling
What exactly is in dispute? Both parties claim absolute ownership (milk mutlaq), meaning ownership without specifying a cause. The question is whose evidence – the possessor's or the outsider's – is stronger in the eyes of the law.
2
State the Hanafi position and its inner logic
The Hanafi school holds that the outsider's evidence is more deserving. Why? Because the hand (yad, possession) is itself a proof of absolute ownership. Therefore, the possessor's evidence merely repeats what is already proven – it is redundant. The outsider's evidence, on the other hand, adds new proof.
This is based on the principle that evidence that creates new information is stronger than evidence that confirms existing information.
3
State the Shafi'i position and its analogy
Imam al-Shafi'i argues that the possessor's evidence should be preferred because it is corroborated by the possession itself. This strengthening of evidence is analogous to cases like proving offspring, marriage, or emancipation, where a natural state (e.g., a child in the house) supports the evidence.
Shafi'i's analogy relies on the idea that corroboration increases the probative value.
4
Analyze the Hanafi rebuttal
The Hanafi school responds that the analogy fails. In the cases of offspring, marriage, etc., the hand (possession) does NOT directly indicate the fact being proved. For example, a man's possession of a child does not prove the child is his offspring. But in absolute ownership, the hand directly indicates ownership. Hence, the possessor's evidence is superfluous, not strengthened.
The key distinction: in the analogues, possession is not a proof of the specific claim; here it is.
5
Note the exception for earlier dating
The rule that the outsider's evidence is preferred is conditioned on the evidence not being dated or the outsider's date being equal or earlier. If the possessor's evidence has an earlier date, judgment is given to the possessor. Also, if the outsider claims absolute ownership and the possessor claims purchase from a third party, the outsider still wins even with an earlier date (as per Al-Zahiriyya).
This exception shows that the Hanafi rule is not absolute; dating can override the preference.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (ولا) بينة لذي اليد في الملك المطلق وبينة الخارج أحق) وقال الشافعي : القاضي يقضي ببينة ذي اليد لاعتضادها باليد فيتقوى الظهور فصار كالنتاج والنكاح وذي الملك الإعتاق والاستيلاد أو التدبير ولنا أن بينة الخارج أكثر إثباتاً وإظهاراً لأن قدر ما أثبته اليد لا يثبته بينة ذي اليد إذ اليد دليل مطلق الملك بخلاف النتاج لأن اليد لا تدل عليه، وكذا على الإعتاق وأختيه وعلى الولاء الثابت بها قيد بالملك المطلق لما سيأتي وأطلقه وهو مقيد بما إذا لم يؤرخا أو أرخا وتاريخ الخارج مساو أو أسبق، أما إذا كان تاريخ ذي اليد أسبق فإنه يقضى له كما سيأتي في الكتاب بخلاف ما إذا ادعى الخارج الملك المطلق وذو اليد الشراء من فلان وبرهنا أورخا وتاريخ ذي اليد أسبق فإنه يقضى للخارج كما في الظهيرية . نفسه فترجح هذا الجانب، يوجب
He said: 'And there is no evidence for the possessor in absolute ownership, and the outsider's evidence is more deserving.' And al-Shafi'i said: The judge decides by the evidence of the possessor because it is supported by the hand (possession), so the appearance is strengthened, becoming like [the evidence for] offspring and marriage and the possessor of [the right of] emancipation, slave-motherhood, or tadbir. Our [argument] is that the evidence of the outsider is more confirming and manifest because what the hand proves, the evidence of the possessor does not prove it, since the hand is a proof of absolute ownership, unlike offspring because the hand does not indicate it, and likewise for emancipation and its sisters, and for the allegiance established by them. It is restricted by absolute ownership as will come. And it is absolute but restricted to when they did not date [their evidences] or they dated and the outsider's date is equal or earlier. As for when the date of the possessor is earlier, then judgment is given for him, as will come in the book. Contrary to when the outsider claims absolute ownership and the possessor claims purchase from someone, and they both present evidence with dates, and the possessor's date is earlier, then judgment is for the outsider, as in Al-Zahiriyya.
Source pages p. 349

Commentary
This is an inter-madhhab dispute. The matn states that the outsider's evidence is preferred over the possessor's evidence in cases of absolute ownership. Imam al-Shafi'i disagrees, arguing that the possessor's evidence is corroborated by his physical possession, strengthening its weight, analogous to evidence for offspring (which is also corroborated by the natural condition) or for marriage or for rights like emancipation. The Hanafi response is that the outsider's evidence is more probative because the hand (possession) already indicates ownership, so the possessor's evidence adds nothing; but the outsider's evidence provides new information. The exception is when the possessor's evidence has an earlier date; then the possessor wins. Also, if the outsider claims absolute ownership and the possessor claims purchase from a third party, even with the possessor's earlier date, judgment goes to the outsider according to Al-Zahiriyya. The passage is cut off at the end.

Key Terms
بينة الخارج (bayyinah al-kharij): The evidence presented by a person who does not have physical possession of the property.
الملك المطلق (al-milk al-mutlaq): Absolute ownership without any specific source or cause.
النتاج (al-nitaaj): Offspring of animals; used as an analogy for evidence corroborated by natural state.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi madhhab, whose evidence is preferred in a claim of absolute ownership?
A) The possessor's evidence
B) The outsider's evidence
C) Both are equal
D) The judge decides arbitrarily

Key Takeaway
In absolute ownership, the outsider's evidence is preferred because possession already proves ownership, unless the possessor's evidence is earlier.

The Legal Effect of Nukul: When Refusal to Swear Binds the Defendant
Detailed commentary on nukul and its legal effects

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions under which nukul (refusal to swear) leads to a judgment against the defendant and identify the exception for a mukatab.

Core Idea
Nukul alone does not bind; only when linked to the judge's ruling does it become effective.

This lesson examines the nuanced ruling on nukul (refusal to take an oath). You will learn that nukul is not automatically decisive — it must be connected to the judge's judgment. We also explore an important exception for a mukatab (slave under contract of manumission), who is not bound by his refusal so that he may preserve his option to annul the contract.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and ruling
What is the act being ruled on? Nukul (refusal to swear). The ruling is that the judge decides against the defendant (qada 'alayhi) after the refusal.
The matn says 'qada lahu' but the lam means 'against'.
2
Understand the rationale (ta'lil)
Why does nukul indicate liability? Because if the defendant were truthful, he would swear to ward off harm. His refusal suggests he is either paying voluntarily (bazil) or confessing (muqirr).
This is a legal presumption (zann).
3
Examine the condition for effect
Nukul alone has no legal force unless it is connected to the judge's ruling (ittisal bi'l-qada). Without that connection, it does not necessitate anything.
This is derived from the chapter of mutual oaths (tahaluf).
4
Apply the exception for a mukatab
If a mukatab refuses to swear regarding his master's claim, nothing is binding on him. This preserves his ability to annul the contract by incapacitation (ta'jiz).
Because the mukatab's nukul would otherwise force him into a binding admission, removing his option.
5
Distinguish the two bases
Nukul can be viewed either as a substitute for an oath (badal) or as an admission (iqrar). As a substitute, its effect depends on the judgment; as an admission, it resembles a substitute and is not independently binding.
This explains why the judicial connection is necessary.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (وقضى له إن نكل مرة بلا أحلف أو سكت) لأن النكول دل على كونه باذلاً أو مقراً إذ لولا ذلك لأقدم على اليمين إقامة للواجب دفعاً للضرر عن ولا وجه لرد اليمين لما قدمناه واللام في «له» بمعنى «على» أي قضى القاضي على المدعى عليه والسكوت لغير آفة دلالة النكول، وذكر الشارح من باب التحالف أن النكول شيئاً إلا إذا اتصل القضاء به وبدونه لا يوجب شيئاً، أما على اعتبار البدل فظاهر، وأما على اعتبار أنه إقرار فلأنه إقرار فيه شبهة البدل فلا يكون موجباً بانفراده اهـ. وذكر بعده أن المكاتب إذا نكل لا يلزمه شيء ليتمكنه من الفسخ بالتعجيز .اهـ. أي إذا نكل عن دعوى السيد
He said: 'And he judged against him if he refused once without swearing or remained silent' because the refusal indicates that he is either paying or confessing, for if it were not so, he would proceed with the oath performing the obligation to repel harm from himself. And there is no way to return the oath as we have stated. And the lam in 'lahu' means 'against', i.e., the judge decides against the defendant. And silence without a defect indicates nukul. And the commentator mentioned from the chapter of mutual oath (tahaluf) that nukul is nothing unless it is connected to the judgment; without that it does not necessitate anything. As for considering it a substitute, it is apparent. As for considering it an admission, it is an admission with the semblance of substitution, so it does not necessitate by itself. And he mentioned afterwards that a mukatab (slave under contract of manumission) if he refuses, nothing is binding upon him to enable him to annul the contract by incapacitation.
Source pages p. 349

Commentary
This passage delves deeper into the nature of nukul (refusal to swear). It explains that nukul is taken as an indication that the denier is either voluntarily paying (bazil) or confessing (muqirr), because if he were truthful, he would swear to avoid harm. The pronoun 'lahu' in the matn is interpreted as 'against him'. The commentator remarks that in the chapter of mutual oaths (tahaluf), nukul alone has no legal effect unless connected to a judgment. If considered a substitute for an oath, it's clear; if considered an admission, it has the semblance of a substitute, so it is not independently binding. An exception is made for a mukatab: if he refuses to swear concerning the master's claim, nothing is imposed on him, allowing him the option to annul the contract.

Key Terms
نكول (nukul): Refusal to take an oath; legally, it can be treated as an admission or lead to a judgment when connected to the judge's ruling.
باذل (bazil): One who voluntarily pays or submits; here, the person who gives in to the claim rather than swearing.
مكاتب (mukatab): A slave who has contracted with his master for manumission in exchange for a payment.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) He must still swear

Key Takeaway
Nukul alone does not bind; it must be connected to the judge's ruling, and the mukatab is an exception.

Swearing a Claimant Without an Opponent: The Cautionary Oath
Refinement of the cautionary oath rule

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on swearing a claimant without a specific opponent and distinguish the two scenarios where the cautionary oath applies or not.

Core Idea
A claimant is sworn cautiously to protect the deceased's right, but only when evidence is based on presumption, not proven payment.

In Hanafi qada, the general rule is that the claimant brings proof and the defendant takes an oath. But what if the defendant is a deceased person with no specific heir or the heir refuses? This passage from Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq addresses a special case: the judge may swear the claimant cautiously (ihtiyatan) to safeguard the deceased's right. The commentator refines this by distinguishing between two types of evidence, showing when caution is needed and when it is not.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Scenario
Recognize the specific situation: a claimant sues the estate of a deceased for a debt, but there is no specific heir opposing, or the heir refuses to take the oath.
The absence of an opponent or their refusal is the trigger for this discussion.
2
State the Ruling
According to al-Ramli, the judge may swear the claimant cautiously (ihtiyatan) because it is the right of the deceased (haqq al-mayyit). This is an exception to the general rule that the claimant is not sworn.
The caution is to avoid injustice; the deceased cannot defend themselves.
3
Understand the Reason for Caution
The cautionary oath is administered because the witnesses may have testified based on istishab al-hal (presumption of continuity) – assuming the debt was never paid. There is a possibility that the deceased actually paid in private, so the oath acts as a safeguard.
Istishab is a weak evidence; thus the oath compensates for uncertainty.
4
Refine the Ruling: The Distinction
The commentator (the author of the passage) refines al-Ramli's view: the cautionary oath is only appropriate when the evidence is based on istishab. If the witnesses testify to the actual payment of the debt (i.e., they directly saw the payment), then there is no uncertainty and the oath is unnecessary.
In the payment case, the evidence is definitive, so caution is not needed.
5
Conclude the Application
Thus, the judge should administer the cautionary oath only when the claimant's evidence is presumptive. If the evidence is direct (actual payment), the judge should not hesitate to swear the claimant, as per al-Ramli, but the refinement shows that hesitation is actually appropriate in the presumptive case.
This refinement preserves the integrity of the oath and prevents misuse.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة : تأمل قوله : (فإنه يحلفه من غير خصم قال الرملي بل وإن أبي الخصم كما صرح به في البزازية معللاً بأنه حق الميت قوله : ( وينبغي أن يحلف احتياطاً قال الرملي ينبغي أن لا يتردد في التحليف أخذاً من قولهم الديون تقضى بأمثالها لا بأعيانها، وإذا كان كذلك فهو قد ادعى حقاً للميت اهـ؛ ذكره الغزي. وأقول: ينبغي أن يقال بدل اللام على كما هو ظاهر وأقول : قد يقال إنما يحلف مدعي الدين على الميت احتياطاً لاحتمال أنهم شهدوا باستصحاب الحال وقد استوفى في باطن الأمر، وأما في مسألة دفع الدين فقد شهدوا على حقيقة الدفع فانتفى الاحتمال المذكور فكيف يقال ينبغي أن لا يتردد في التحليف تأمل قوله : فكيف (الشاهد ظاهره أن التحليف للشاهد وظاهر ما قبله أن التحليف للمدعي على صدق الشاهد تأمل قوله : ( ولا وجه لرد اليمين) أي على المدعي وقوله «لما قدمناه» إشارة لقوله ولا ترد اليمين على المدعي لقوله عليه السلام البينة على المدعي الخ.
Issue: Consider his saying: 'Then he swears him without an opponent.' Al-Ramli said: Rather, even if the opponent refuses, as stated in Al-Bazzaziyya, reasoning that it is the right of the deceased. His saying: 'And it is appropriate that he swears cautiously.' Al-Ramli said: There should be no hesitation in administering the oath, taking from their saying: Debts are discharged by their likes, not by their exact things. And if so, then he has claimed a right for the deceased. Mentioned by al-Ghazzi. And I say: It would be appropriate to say instead of the lam, as is apparent. And I say: It may be said that the claimant of a debt against the deceased is only sworn cautiously due to the possibility that they testified based on the continuity of the condition, and he may have actually received it. As for the case of payment of the debt, they testified to the actual fact of payment, so the mentioned possibility is removed. So how can it be said that there should be no hesitation in administering the oath? Consider his saying: 'How can the witness?' It appears that the oath is for the witness, and the apparent sense of what precedes it is that the oath is for the claimant regarding the truthfulness of the witness. Consider his saying: 'And there is no way to return the oath' meaning to the claimant, and his saying 'as we have stated' refers to his saying: 'And the oath is not returned to the claimant' because of his saying, peace be upon him: 'The burden of proof is on the claimant...'
Source pages p. 349

Commentary
This is a discussion on the permissibility of swearing a claimant when there is no specific opponent, such as a claim against the deceased's estate. Al-Ramli argues that the judge may swear the claimant even if the opponent (the heirs) refuses, because it is the right of the deceased. The author (the commentator) raises a point: in cases where the claimant's evidence is about the actual payment of a debt, the need for caution is less, but in cases of a claim on the estate, caution is needed because the witnesses may have testified based on the presumption of continuity. The discussion ties back to the principle that the oath is not returned to the claimant, but here the judge administers it for caution, not as a return. The passage ends with a reference to the hadith.

Key Terms
بدل اللام على (badal al-lam 'ala): A commentary phrase discussing whether the lam (preposition) should be interpreted as 'against' or something else.
احتياطاً (ihtiyatan): As a precaution; the oath is administered for caution, not as an obligation.
استصحاب الحال (istishab al-hal): Presumption of continuity; a legal presumption that a state of affairs continues until proven otherwise.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
The cautionary oath for a claimant without an opponent is permissible but only when the evidence is presumptive, not when it is direct.

Intra-Hanafi Dispute on Administering Oaths in the Seven Matters
Intra-Hanafi dispute with fatwa resolution

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the intra-Hanafi dispute regarding administering oaths in the 'seven matters' and apply the mufta bihi position based on the judge's discretion.

Core Idea
Abu Hanifa says no oath; the Sahibayn say yes. Later Hanafis give the judge discretion based on the defendant's character.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq delves into an important procedural dispute within the Hanafi school: should a judge administer an oath to the defendant in claims like lineage, marriage, and guardianship? The difference between Imam Abu Hanifa and his two disciples, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani (the Sahibayn), is resolved by later scholars through a discretionary approach. Let us trace the reasoning and practical application.

Guided Path
1
Identify the disputed issue
The core issue is whether the defendant (munkir) should be administered an oath (istihlaf) in claims known as the 'six (or seven) things' — claims that do not primarily involve wealth, such as lineage, marriage, guardianship, etc.
'Six things' is the classical expression; later texts include a seventh (motherhood of child).
2
State the positions of Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn
Abu Hanifa holds that no oath is administered because these matters are based on lineage, which cannot be fully verified by oath. The Sahibayn hold that the oath should be administered to protect against false denials.
The Sahibayn are Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani.
3
Analyze the tarjih (preference) of later scholars
Later Hanafi masters, such as al-Bazdawi and those after him, reconciled the two views by giving the judge discretion: if the judge sees the defendant as obstinate (muta'annit), he administers the oath (Sahibayn); if he sees him as oppressed (mazlum), he does not (Abu Hanifa).
This is the mufta bihi position — the operative ruling for fatwa.
4
Examine the practical examples from the text
The passage provides examples: the form of the oath in marriage cases (conditional divorce), the distinction between lineage that is established without wealth (sonship, marriage) versus lineage that requires a wealth claim (brotherhood, unclehood), and the stratagem (hila) to repel the oath.
These examples show the complexity of applying the general rule.
5
Understand the thamarah al-khilaf (practical consequence)
The practical difference is that the judge must assess the defendant's demeanor. This requires judicial insight and is not a mechanical rule. The passage also shows how claims for brotherhood alone (without wealth) are problematic: some texts accept the claim, others reject it because it effectively claims sonship to the father.
The khilaf directly affects litigation strategy.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الدعوى ما القاضي الإمام فخر الدين الفتوى على أنه يستحلف المنكر في الأشياء الستة» المراد به مولانا قاضيخان كما صرح به مسكين وعزاه المصنف له في شرح الجامع الصغير أنه مع صرح به في فتاواه أيضاً، وصرح الشارح بأن فخر الإسلام علي البزدوي اختار قولهما للفتوى على ما ذكره في المختصر، واختار المتأخرون من مشايخنا على أن القاضي ينظر في حال المدعى عليه، فإن رآه متعنتاً يحلفه أخذاً بقولهما وإن رآه مظلوماً لا يحلفه أخذا بقول أبي حنيفة. وفي الولوالجية : الفتوى على قولهما وهو اختيار الفقيه أبي الليث وصورة الاستحلاف على قولهما هي بزوجة لي وإن كانت زوجة لي فهي طالق بائن لأنها لو كانت صادقة لا يبطل النكاح بجحوده فإذا حلف تبقى معطلة. وقال بعضهم : يستحلف على النكاح فإن حلف يقول القاضي فرقت بينكما كذا في الخانية وفي الاختيار ثم عندهما كل نسب يثبت من غير دعوى المال كالبنوة والزوجية والمال يستحلف عليه، وكل نسب لو أقر به لا يثبت إلا بدعوى المال كالأخ والعم لا يستحلف إلا إذا ادعى بسببه مالاً أو حقاً كدعوى الإرث ودعوى عدم الرجوع في الهبة ونحوه .اهـ. وظاهره صحة الدعوى بنسب الأخ ونحوه وإن لم يدع المال لأنه إنما نفى الاستحلاف فقط، وظاهر ما في البزازية من الفصل العاشر في النسب والإرث عدم صحة الدعوى بالأخوة المجردة ولهذا لو برهن لا يقبل لأنه في الحقيقة إثبات البنوة على أب المدعى عليه والخصم فيه هو الأب لا الأخ .اهـ. وفي شرح مسكين فإن قيل : كيف تكون هذه المسائل ستة وهي سبعة؟ قلنا أمومية الولد تابعة لثبوت النسب .اهـ. وعبر عنها في جامع الفصولين بالأشياء السبعة وفيه : ادعى نكاحها فحيلة دفع اليمين عنها على قولهما أن تتزوج فلا تحلف لأنها لو نكلت لا يحكم عليها لأنها لو أقرت بعد ما تزوجت لم يجز إقرارها، وكذا لو أقرت بنكاح لغائب قيل صح إقرارها لكن يبطل بالتكذيب ويندفع عنها اليمين، وقيل لا يصح إقرارها فلا تندفع عنها اليمين .اهـ. وفي الولوالجية : رجل تزوج امرأة بشهادة شاهدين ثم أنكرت وتزوجت بآخر ومات شهود الأول ليس للزوج الأول أن يخاصمها لأنها للتحليف والمقصود منه النكول، ولو أقرت صريحاً لم يجز إقرارها لكن يخاصم الزوج الثاني ويحلفه، فإن حلف برئ، وإن نكل فله أن يخاصمها ويحلفها، فإن نكلت يقضى بها للمدعي وهذا الجواب على قولهما المفتى به اهـ .
Book of Litigation: What the Qadi Imam Fakhr al-Din issued a fatwa that the defendant is administered an oath in the six things. What is meant by it is our master Qadi Khan, as stated by Miskin and attributed to him by the author in Sharh al-Jami' al-Saghir that he also stated it in his Fatawa. And the commentator stated that Fakhr al-Islam 'Ali al-Bazdawi chose their view (Sahibayn) for fatwa based on what he mentioned in al-Mukhtasar. And the later scholars among our masters chose that the judge looks at the situation of the defendant: if he sees him as obstinate, he administers the oath adopting the view of the Sahibayn; if he sees him as oppressed, he does not administer the oath adopting the view of Abu Hanifa. In al-Walwaljiyah: the fatwa is on their view, and it is the choice of al-Faqih Abu al-Layth. The form of administering the oath according to them is by saying 'if you are my wife, then you are divorced with a bain divorce,' because if she were truthful, the marriage would not be nullified by his denial, so if he swears, she remains idle. And some said: he is administered an oath on the marriage; if he swears, the judge says 'I have separated the two of you.' Thus in al-Khaniyah and al-Ikhtiyar. Then according to them, every lineage that is established without a claim of wealth, like sonship and marriage, an oath is administered concerning it, and every lineage that if one acknowledges it is not established except through a claim of wealth, like brotherhood and unclehood, an oath is not administered unless one claims by it wealth or a right, like a claim of inheritance and a claim of non-return of a gift, etc. End quote. Its apparent meaning is the validity of a claim of brotherhood lineage etc. even if he does not claim wealth, because he only negated the administration of the oath. But what is apparent in al-Bazzaziyyah from the tenth chapter on lineage and inheritance is the invalidity of a claim of mere brotherhood, and therefore if he presents evidence, it is not accepted, because in reality it is establishing sonship to the father of the defendant, and the opponent is the father, not the brother. End quote. In Sharh Miskin: if it is said, how are these issues six while they are seven? He said: the motherhood of the child is dependent on the establishment of lineage. End quote. And they are expressed in Jami' al-Fuslayn as the seven things. In it: if he claims her marriage, then the stratagem for repelling the oath from her according to them is that she should get married, because if she refuses, judgment is not passed against her, because if she acknowledges after she got married, her acknowledgment is not valid. Similarly, if she acknowledges a marriage to an absent person, it was said her acknowledgment is valid but is nullified by denial, and the oath is repelled from her. And it was said her acknowledgment is not valid, so the oath is not repelled from her. End quote. In al-Walwaljiyah: a man married a woman with the testimony of two witnesses, then she denied and married another, and the first's witnesses died. The first husband has no right to litigate against her because she is for the purpose of a sworn oath, and the purpose is refusal. If she explicitly acknowledges, her acknowledgment is not valid, but he litigates against the second husband and administers the oath to him. If he swears, he is cleared. If he refuses, then he has the right to litigate against her and administer the oath to her. If she refuses, she is adjudged to the claimant. This answer is according to their view, which is the relied upon fatwa. End quote.
Source pages p. 355

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute concerning whether the judge should administer an oath to the defendant in certain types of claims. The 'six things' (or seven) refer to claims of lineage, marriage, guardianship, etc., where the defendant's denial is not easily verifiable. Imam Abu Hanifa held that no oath should be administered in these cases, while Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani (the Sahibayn) held that an oath should be administered. The text presents the adopted fatwa position: later scholars differentiated based on the judge's assessment—if the defendant appears obstinate, he is oathed (Sahibayn's view); if he appears oppressed, he is not (Abu Hanifa's view). The passage then provides examples: the form of the oath in marriage cases (using a conditional divorce), the distinction between lineage that is established without wealth (like sonship) versus lineage that requires a wealth claim (like brotherhood), and various scenarios involving marriage denials and stratagems to repel the oath. The text concludes with a ruling from al-Walwaljiyah about a case where a woman denies marriage and remarries; the first husband must litigate against the second husband, and if he refuses to swear, the woman is adjudged to the claimant.

Key Terms
الاستحلاف (al-istihlaf): The act of administering a sworn oath to a party in litigation.
المنكر (al-munkir): The defendant who denies the claim.
النكول (al-nukul): The refusal of a party to take an oath after being requested.
الأشياء الستة (al-ashya' al-sitta): The six (or seven) types of claims where the defendant should not be oathed according to Abu Hanifa.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) The judge's assessment of the defendant (obstinate or oppressed)
C) The presence of witnesses
D) The plaintiff's request for an oath

Key Takeaway
In the Hanafi school, the judge's discretion based on the defendant's character resolves the dispute between Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn on administering oaths in the seven matters.

Thirty-One Cases Where No Oath Is Administered
List of thirty-one cases where Abu Hanifa holds no oath is administered

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to list the categories of cases where Abu Hanifa rules no oath is administered in litigation, and identify the disputes with the Sahibayn in specific instances.

Core Idea
No oath is administered in marriages by father/master, agency claims, double property claims, artisan disputes, and agency for the absent.

The author of Kanz al-Daqaiq mentioned only seven cases where no oath is administered. The commentator expands this to thirty-one, some agreed upon and some disputed. This list is a key memorization anchor for Hanafi qada (judicial procedure). Understanding these cases helps a judge know when to refrain from administering an oath and highlights differences between Abu Hanifa and his two companions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the rule and its scope
Recognize that the ruling is about the judge not administering an oath (istihlaf) to a defendant who denies a claim. The list provides specific exceptions to a general rule that an oath is required.
The general rule is that a defendant is sworn when denying a claim, unless the case is in this list.
2
Categorize the cases
Group the thirty-one cases into categories: family law (marriage by father/master), agency/executor claims, property disputes (two claimants on same item: purchase, gift, pawn, lease, charity, deposit, loan), artisan vs. employer, and agency for an absent person.
Each category has a common rationale; understanding the rationale helps memorization.
3
Note intra-madhhab differences
For several cases, Abu Hanifa differs from the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad). For example, in giving a minor daughter in marriage, Abu Hanifa says no oath, but the Sahibayn say the father is sworn. Identify these points of ikhtilaf.
These differences affect the operative ruling in some jurisdictions.
4
Analyze the thirty-first case and al-Halwani's objection
The thirty-first case involves an agent for an absent person claiming debt; Abu Hanifa says no oath, but al-Halwani reports that the debtor is sworn according to all scholars. Understand that this is a disputed point even within the Hanafi school.
This shows the dynamic nature of the school; later scholars may refine positions.
5
Understand the criticism of al-Khulasa
The commentator criticizes al-Khulasa for stating that a defendant is always sworn except in three cases. The correct Hanafi position is that there are many exceptions, as listed here.
This highlights the importance of comprehensive source study.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم اعلم أن المصنف اقتصر على عدم الاستحلاف عنده في الأشياء السبعة، وفي الخانية أنه لا استحلاف في إحدى وثلاثين خصلة بعضها مختلف فيه وبعضها متفق عليه فنذكرها سرداً اختصاراً : السبعة، وفي تزويج البنت صغيرة أو كبيرة وعندهما يستحلف الأب في الصغيرة، وفي تزويج المولى أمته خلافاً لهما، وفي دعوى الدائن الإيصاء فأنكره لا يحلف، وفي دعوى الدين على الوصي، وفي الدعوى على الوكيل في المسألتين كالوصي، وفيما إذا كان في يد ء فادعاه رجلان كل الشراء منه فأقر به لأحدهما وأنكر الآخر لا يحلفه، وكذا لو أنكرهما فحلف لأحدهما فنكل له وقضى عليه لم يحلف للآخر، وفيما إذا ادعيا الهبة مع التسليم من ذي اليد فأقر لأحدهما لا يحلف للآخر، وكذا لو نكل لأحدهما لا يحلف للآخر، وفيما إذا ادعى كل منهما أنه رهنه وقبضه فأقر به لأحدهما أو حلف لأحدهما فنكل لا يحلف للآخر، وفيما إذا ادعى أحدهما الرهن والتسليم والآخر الشراء فأقر بالرهن وأنكر البيع لا يحلف للمشتري، وفيما إذا ادعى أحد رجلين الإجارة والآخر الشراء فأقر بها وأنكره لا يحلف لمدعيه ويقال لمدعيه إن شئت فانتظر انقضاء المدة وفك الرهن وإن شئت فافسخ، وفيما إذا ادعى أحدهما الصدقة والقبض والآخر الشراء فأقر لأحدهما لا يستحلف للثاني، وفيما إذا ادعى كل منهما الإجارة فأقر لأحدهما أو نكل لا يحلف للآخر بخلاف ما إذا ادعى كل منهما على ذي اليد الغصب منه فأقر لأحدهما أو حلف لأحدهما فنكل يحلف للثاني كما لو ادعى كل منهما الإيداع فأقر لأحدهما يحلف للثاني، وكذا الإعارة ويحلف ما له عليك كذا ولا قيمته كذا وكذا، وفيما إذا ادعى البائع رضا الموكل بالعيب لم يحلف وكيله، وفيما إذا أنكر توكيله له في النكاح وفيما اختلف الصانع والمستصنع في المأمور به لا يمين على واحد منهما، وكذا لو ادعى الصانع على رجل أنه استصنعه في كذا فأنكر لا يحلف الحادية والثلاثون لو ادعى أنه وكيل عن الغائب بقبض دينه وبالخصومة فأنكر لا يستحلف المديون على قوله خلافاً لهما هكذا ذكر بعضهم. وقال الحلواني : يستحلف في قولهم جميعاً اهـ. وبه علم أن ما في الخلاصة تساهل وقصور حيث قال : كل موضع لو أقر لزمه فإذا أنكره يستحلف إلا في ثلاث مسائل منها الوكيل بالشراء إذا وجد بالمشتري عيباً فأراد أن يرده وهي قوله : (وفي دعوى الدائن الإيصاء) أي أن فلاناً وصي عن الميت قوله : (رضي الموكل) أي موكل المشتري قوله : (الثانية) لو ادعى على الأمر رضاه الخ صورته اشترى شيئاً بالوكالة فظهر به
Then know that the author confined himself to no oath in his view in the seven things. In al-Khaniyah, there is no oath in thirty-one characteristics, some of which are disputed and some agreed upon. We will mention them in sequence briefly: the seven; and in giving a daughter in marriage, whether minor or adult—according to them the father is sworn in the minor; in the master giving his slave-girl in marriage, contrary to them; in the creditor's claim of appointment of a representative (wasiyya), and he denies, he is not sworn; in the claim of debt against the executor; in the claim against the agent in two issues like the executor; and in the case where someone has something in his hand and two men each claim purchase from him, he acknowledges to one and denies the other, he does not swear the other; similarly, if he denies both and swears to one and the other refuses and judgment is passed against him, he is not sworn for the other; and if two claim a gift with delivery from the possessor, he acknowledges to one, he does not swear the other; similarly, if one refuses, he does not swear the other; and if each claims that he pawned and received possession, and he acknowledges to one or swears for one and the other refuses, he does not swear the other; and if one claims pawn and delivery and the other claims purchase, and he acknowledges the pawn and denies the sale, he does not swear the purchaser; and if one of two claims lease and the other claims purchase, and he acknowledges it and denies him, he does not swear the claimant, and it is said to the claimant: if you wish, wait for the expiry of the period and redemption of the pawn, and if you wish, annul; and if one claims charity and receipt and the other claims purchase, and he acknowledges one, he is not sworn for the second; and if each claims lease, and he acknowledges one or refuses, he does not swear the other, contrary to the case where each claims usurpation from the possessor, and he acknowledges one or swears for one and the other refuses, he swears the second, as when each claims deposit and he acknowledges one, he swears the second; similarly for lending, and he swears 'you have nothing against me of this amount nor its value'; and if the seller claims the principal's approval of the defect, his agent is not sworn; and if he denies his agency in marriage, and in the dispute between the artisan and the employer regarding the ordered work, there is no oath on either; similarly, if the artisan claims against a man that he commissioned him for such work and he denies, he is not sworn — the thirty-first: if he claims that he is agent for the absent person to receive his debt and for litigation, and the debtor denies, the debtor is not sworn according to his view, contrary to them. Thus some have mentioned. And al-Halwani said: he is sworn according to all their views. End quote. And from this it is known that what is in al-Khulasa is lenient and deficient where it said: every place where if he acknowledges he is bound, then if he denies he is sworn, except in three issues, among them the agent by purchase if he finds a defect in the buyer and wants to return it. And it is his saying: (And in the creditor's claim of appointment) meaning that so-and-so is an executor for the deceased; his saying: (the principal's approval) meaning the principal of the buyer; his saying: (the second) if he claims against the commander his approval, etc. Its form: he bought something by agency and a defect appeared in it.
Source pages p. 356

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement that lists thirty-one cases where, according to Abu Hanifa, no oath is administered to the defendant when he denies a claim. The list begins with the seven things (the original six plus one) and then enumerates many specific scenarios: marriages involving a father or master, claims against executors and agents, disputes over property possession, gifts, pawns, sales, leases, charity, deposits, loans, defects in goods, agency in marriage, artisan contracts, and agency for absent persons. In each case, the rule is that if the defendant denies, he is not sworn. The text also notes differences between Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn in some cases, and includes an opinion from al-Halwani that in the last case (agent for absent person) the debtor is sworn according to all. The passage criticizes al-Khulasa for being lenient by stating that a defendant is always sworn except in three issues, whereas the correct list is much longer. The list ends with comments on specific items from the original text.

Key Terms
الاستحلاف (al-istihlaf): Administering an oath to a party in litigation.
المصنف (al-musannif): The author of the matn, likely the author of Kanz al-Daqaiq (Abu al-Barakat al-Nasafi).
الخصلة (al-khasla): A characteristic or case; here it refers to each specific legal situation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the list in this passage, if an artisan claims that a man commissioned him for work and the man denies, is the artisan sworn?
A) Yes, because it is a financial claim
B) No, according to Abu Hanifa no oath is administered in such a case
C) Only if the value exceeds a certain amount
D) The judge decides

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school recognizes at least thirty-one specific cases where a judge must not administer an oath, with some disagreements between Abu Hanifa and his companions.

10 min
Exceptions to Oath in Financial Claims
Listing exceptions to oath in financial claims and explaining the husband case

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the cases in which the defendant is not required to take an oath in financial claims and state the effect of admission (iqrar) and refusal (nukul) in those cases according to the Hanafi school.

Core Idea
Oath is not administered in several agency, guardianship, and specific claim scenarios; but admission is always binding, and refusal leads to liability when the claim is financial.

In Hanafi law, the default rule is that a defendant must swear an oath when the plaintiff lacks evidence. However, this passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq lists exceptions where the oath is not administered. These include certain agency disputes, guardianship matters, and specific claims like defect in sale, witness retraction, and theft. Understanding these exceptions is crucial for correctly applying procedural rules in financial litigation.

Guided Path
1
Recognize the default rule
Recall that in financial claims, if the plaintiff has no proof, the judge may require the defendant to swear an oath (istihlaf). Refusal (nukul) is treated as quasi-confession leading to liability.
This default is modified by the exceptions in the text.
2
Identify the three agency exceptions
Locate the three cases where the agent is not sworn: 1) seller's agent when principal denies knowledge of defect, 2) agent when satisfaction of principal is claimed, 3) agent for debt collection when debtor claims principal discharged him.
In each, if the agent admits, the admission binds him; refusal leads to liability only if admission does not occur.
3
List the additional exceptions
Find the other cases: seller denying existence of defect at present (according to Abu Hanifa), witness denying retraction, thief denying theft (to avoid amputation), and guardians (father, guardian, trustee) not being sworn unless a contract is claimed against them.
Note that in these, admission (iqrar) triggers the full consequence (e.g., liability, amputation).
4
Analyze the husband case and underlying principle
Examine the example of a wife claiming divorce before consummation: the husband is sworn if the claim involves property (dowry, maintenance). The principle is that oath is administered when the objective is financial, even if the underlying act (like divorce) is non-financial.
This shows the rule extends to claims for inheritance, custody, maintenance, etc. when they involve property.
5
Summarize the effect of admission and refusal
In all exceptions, if the defendant admits (iqrar), the admission is binding and has legal effect. If the defendant refuses to swear (nukul) in cases where oath would normally be administered, liability follows. In the exceptions, nukul does not automatically impose liability because the oath is not administered, but if the case involves a financial right, refusal may still lead to liability through other means.
This distinction is critical for understanding the procedural strategy.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
نكل ضمن ولم يقطع والزوج إن ادعت المرأة طلاقاً قبل الوطء فإن نكل ضمن نصف بالعيب وأراد البائع أن يحلفه بالله ما يعلم أن الموكل رضي بالعيب لا يحلف، فإن أقر الوكيل لزمه ذلك ويبطل حق الرد الثانية لو ادعى على الآمر رضاه لا يحلف وإن أقر لزمه الثالثة الوكيل بقبض الدين إذا ادعى المديون أن الموكل أبرأه عن الدين وطلب يمين الوكيل على العلم لا يحلف وإن أقر به لزمه .اهـ. وزدت على الواحدة والثلاثين السابقة البائع إذا أنكر قيام العيب للحال لا يحلف عند الإمام، ولو أقر به لزمه كما قدمناه في خيار العيب، والشاهد إذا أنكر رجوعه لا يستحلف، ولو أقر به ضمن ما تلف بها، والسارق إذا أنكرها لا يستحلف للقطع ولو أقر بها قطع، وذكر الأسبيجابي : ولا يستحلف الأب في مال الصبي ولا الوصي في مال اليتيم ولا المتولي للمسجد والأوقاف إلا إذا ادعى عليهم العقد يستحلفون حينئذ اهـ . قوله : ( والزوج إن ادعت المرأة طلاقاً قبل الوطء فإن نكل ضمن نصف المهر) لأن الاستحلاف يجري في الطلاق عندهم لا سيما إذا كان المقصود هو المال . أشار المؤلف إلى أن الاستحلاف في المواضع السابقة يجري عند دعوى المال فيحلف في النكاح إذا ادعت هي الصداق لأنه دعوى المال، ثم يثبت المال بنكوله ولا يثبت النكاح، وبه علم أنه لا فائدة في تقييد المؤلف المسألة بالطلاق قبل الدخول إذ لا فرق بين أن يكون قبل الطلاق أو بعده قبل الدخول أو بعده في الاستحلاف كما في النهاية، ولا فرق بين أن تدعي المهر أو نفقة العدة كما في الخانية، وكذا في النسب إذا ادعى حقاً كالإرث والحجر في اللقيط والنفقة وامتناع الرجوع في الهبة لأن المقصود في هذه الحقوق هو المال وبيان صور هذه الأربعة في النهاية
He refused to swear and is liable and it is not cut off. And the husband: if the woman claims divorce before intercourse, then if he refuses to swear he is liable for half of the dowry with the defect, and if the seller wants to make him swear by Allah that he knows that the principal was satisfied with the defect, he is not made to swear. If the agent admits, it is binding on him and the right of rejection is void. The second: if someone claims the principal's satisfaction, he is not sworn, and if he admits it is binding on him. The third: the agent for receiving debt: if the debtor claims that the principal discharged him from the debt and requests the oath of the agent on knowledge, he is not sworn, and if he admits it, it is binding on him. End. And I added to the previous thirty-one: the seller if he denies the existence of the defect at present is not sworn according to Imam, and if he admits it, it is binding on him as we mentioned in option of defect. And the witness if he denies his retraction is not sworn, and if he admits retraction, he guarantees what was lost by it. And the thief if he denies the theft is not sworn for amputation, and if he admits it, he is amputated. And al-Asbijabi mentioned: The father is not sworn regarding the property of the minor, nor the guardian regarding the property of the orphan, nor the trustee of the mosque and endowments, except if a contract is claimed against them, then they are sworn at that time. End. His statement: (And the husband if the woman claims divorce before intercourse, then if he refuses to swear, he is liable for half the dowry) because administering an oath occurs in divorce according to them, especially when the objective is property. The author indicated that administering an oath in previous instances occurs in claims of property, so he is sworn in marriage if she claims the dowry because it is a property claim. Then property is established by his refusal, but marriage is not established. By this it is known that there is no benefit in the author restricting the issue to divorce before consummation, since there is no difference whether it is before or after divorce, before or after consummation in admininstering the oath, as in al-Nihayah. And there is no difference whether she claims the dowry or the maintenance during the waiting period, as in al-Khaniyah. Likewise in lineage, if someone claims a right such as inheritance, custody of a foundling, maintenance, and prohibition of revoking a gift, because the objective in these rights is property. And the explanation of these four is in al-Nihayah.
Source pages p. 357

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the matn of Kanz al-Daqa'iq, listing exceptions to the general rule that a defendant can be required to take an oath in financial claims. The author adds three cases to the thirty-one previously mentioned. In agency cases: (1) in a sale with defect, the principal is not made to swear about knowledge of the defect; if the agent admits, it binds him and voids the right of rejection. (2) If someone claims the principal's satisfaction, again no oath, but admission binds. (3) The agent for debt collection: if debtor claims discharge, agent not sworn on knowledge; admission binds. Additional cases: a seller denying present defect (according to Abu Hanifa), a witness denying retraction, and a thief denying theft (to avoid amputation). If they admit, consequences apply. Also, guardians (father, guardian, trustee) are not sworn unless a contract is claimed. Then the author explains the husband case: if wife claims divorce before consummation and husband refuses to swear, he must pay half the dowry. This is because the oath is used in financial matters; even though the claim involves marriage, the objective is property. The rule applies whether divorce occurred before or after consummation, and also covers claims for maintenance during iddah, inheritance, etc. The key principle is that oath is administered when the claim is ultimately financial.

Key Terms
نكول (nukūl): Refusal of the defendant to take the oath
إقرار (iqrār): Admission or confession by a party in a legal claim
استحلاف (istiḥlāf): The act of the judge administering an oath to the defendant

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, in which of the following cases is the defendant NOT required to take an oath?
A) A husband when the wife claims maintenance during the waiting period after divorce.
B) An agent in debt collection when the debtor claims the principal discharged him.
C) A seller when the buyer claims a defect in the sold item.
D) A guardian of an orphan when someone claims a right of inheritance.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, the oath is not administered in specific agency, guardianship, and other exceptional cases, but admission always binds and refusal (nukul) leads to liability only when the oath is required.

Oaths on Knowledge vs Certainty in Property Claims
Defines when oaths are on knowledge vs certainty based on possession source

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state which type of oath is required for an heir versus a buyer/giftee and explain the legal rationale.

Core Idea
Oath on knowledge for inherited property; oath on certainty for purchased/gifted property.

When a person is sued for property, the form of the oath he takes is not arbitrary—it reflects his relationship to the claim. If he inherited the property, he swears on knowledge ('I do not know it is yours'), because he only knows what the decedent left. If he bought or received it as a gift, he swears on certainty ('This is mine'), because he personally acquired it. This ruling from Kanz al-Daqaiq highlights the precision of Hanafi procedural law.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two property sources
The passage distinguishes between inherited property and property acquired by purchase or gift. Note these two categories.
2
Link each to the appropriate oath type
For inheritance, the oath is on knowledge (yamin 'ala al-'ilm). For purchase/gift, it is on certainty (yamin 'ala al-batat). Memorise this correlation.
3
Understand the rationale behind the distinction
An heir does not have firsthand knowledge of the decedent's transactions, so his oath can only be that he does not know the claim to be true. A purchaser has direct knowledge of his own acquisition, so his oath must be definitive.
4
Note the authority for this rule
Al-Khassaf and al-Sadr al-Shahid are cited as proponents, indicating this is the relied-upon Hanafi position (zahir al-riwayah).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
على السبب وعلى العلم لو ورث عبداً فادعاه آخر وعلى البتات لو وهب له أو اشتراه
على نفس الإيلاء إذا قالت إنه لا يرى الوقوع بمضي المدة. ثم اعلم أن ظاهر ما ذكره الخصاف وتبعه الصدر الشهيد أن معرفة كون المدعي شافعياً ونحوه إنما هي بقول المدعي، ولم أر حكم ما إذا تنازعا في ذلك وظاهر كلامهما أنه لا اعتبار بقول المدعى عليه
On the cause and on knowledge: if he inherited a slave and another claims it, [the oath is] on knowledge; and on certainty if it was gifted to him or he bought it. On the same [basis] as ɪla, when she says that he does not see its occurrence with the passing of time. Then know that the apparent understanding of what al-Khassaf mentioned, and followed by al-Sadr al-Shahid, is that knowing whether the plaintiff is a Shafi'i and the like is only by the statement of the plaintiff. I have not seen a ruling if they dispute over that, and the apparent meaning of their statements is that no consideration is given to the statement of the defendant.
Source pages p. 369

Commentary
This is a ruling statement from the matn of Kanz al-Daqaiq, commented upon by the author. The passage sets out a key distinction in oaths in claims: when property is inherited, the defendant (heir) swears on knowledge (i.e., 'I do not know that this belongs to the claimant'), because they have no direct knowledge of the decedent's transactions. When property is acquired by purchase or gift, the defendant swears on certainty (i.e., 'This is mine definitively'), because they are claiming ownership based on their own act. The commentary adds a nuance: if the plaintiff claims to follow a madhhab that might affect the ruling, his own statement is taken into account, not the defendant's.

Key Terms
يمين على العلم (yamin 'ala al-'ilm): An oath where the defendant swears 'I do not know' regarding the claim, used when the claim involves acts of others (e.g., decedent).
يمين على البتات (yamin 'ala al-batat): A definitive oath where the defendant swears 'this is not so' with certainty, used for one's own acts.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a person inherits a slave and another person claims the slave is his, what type of oath does the heir swear?
A) Oath on certainty (على البتات)
B) Oath on knowledge (على العلم)
C) No oath required
D) Oath on cause (على السبب)

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school distinguishes oath types based on the defendant's relationship to the property: knowledge for inherited, certainty for purchased/gifted.
